Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
8.1  General Overview of Drug Development
Toxicology has a long history in providing a path forward 
in the assessment of adverse effects and the perspective that 
adverse effects can be managed differently in various dis-
eases and patient populations. Fogelman [1] reminds us that 
toxicology “… reaches into almost all of the disciplines of 
science to provide knowledge for the protection and preserva-
tion of man”. Hamlin [2] has reminded us of the advice of Dr. 
Zbinden (with two Hamlin corollaries), when in search for an 
understanding of risk:
•	 Do not do something just because you can.
•	 Do not do something just because it has always been 
done.
•	 Do not do something just because others do it.
•	 Do not do something because (you believe) it is 
expected.
•	 Do not do something the results of which cannot be 
interpreted.
A drug has been defined in many ways, and by many gov-
ernmental agencies. The World Health Organization (WHO) 
Scientiﬁc Group has deﬁned a drug as “any substance or prod-
uct that is used or intended to be used to modify or explore 
physiological systems or pathological states for the beneﬁt 
of the recipient” [3]. The process of drug discovery/develop-
ment covers a wide range of therapeutic areas and treatment 
regimens and is a risky, multifaceted, expensive undertaking. 
The goal is to develop a new product with therapeutic beneﬁts 
(efﬁcacy) and few or manageable side effects (toxicity) [4]. In 
the simplest terms, the drug discovery/development process, 
for a new chemical entity (NCE), starts at the chemist’s com-
puter with in silico generation and testing of theoretical mol-
ecules, followed by synthesis of molecules of interest, and then 
testing, through various in vitro and in vivo pharmacology and 
toxicology models, including pharmacologic proﬁling (the 
determination of pharmacologic effects other than the desired 
therapeutic effect, i.e., off-target-of-interest effects), based on 
the proposed clinical plan for the ﬁrst human dose (FHD), also 
termed first-in-human (FIH) dose.
The concepts involved in the basic approach to nonclinical 
safety testing have evolved and have been covered in numer-
ous publications over more than 50 years. A basic introduc-
tory approach to drug development has been presented at 
an American College of Toxicology workshop [5] covering 
aspects of chemistry, manufacturing, and control; toxicology; 
clinical trials and regulatory interactions. The principal aim 
of nonclinical safety testing continues to be the understanding 
of safety/efficacy of the drug candidate well enough to make 
a judgment that the risk/beneﬁt proﬁle is adequate to progress 
a potential drug candidate toward clinical trials [6]. Provided 
the efﬁcacy pharmacology and initial toxicology proﬁles are 
acceptable, clinical safety, pharmacokinetic, and pharmacody-
namic studies (Phase I studies) can be initiated. As the human 
clinical trials progress through Phase II (proof of concept and 
safety studies) and Phase III (pivotal registration studies), the 
drug candidate moves through nonclinical subchronic studies, 
chronic and developmental toxicology studies, and oncogenic 
evaluations. Gerhard Zbinden [7], a major contributor to the 
field of toxicology, has provided a summary of the biological 
parameters that should be evaluated for new drug candidates 
(Table 8.1), these recommendations are relevant today but are 
not static and can be adjusted as needed.
Accelerating the development of safe and effective drugs is 
not a new topic to the pharmaceutical industry [8,9], advances 
have been made and will continue to be made in the cycle 
time of the overall drug development process; however, the 
basic principles of safety and efficacy continue to drive the 
process. The technical risks in new drug development pro-
grams are enormous. Drug development is complicated by the 
requirement to simultaneously address complex issues related 
to potency, selectivity, reversibility, solubility, duration, meta-
bolic stability, permeability, toxicity, physical stability, patent-
ability, and manufacturability for each drug candidate. The 
risk of failure related to one or more of these aspects has been 
reviewed by Chien [10], where it was reported that <0.02% of 
NCEs result in marketed drug products, and even fewer (i.e., 
0.002%) return a proﬁt to support continued drug research 
(Figure 8.1). Very little of what enters the drug development 
pipeline ever enters the marketplace [11]. It has been estimated 
through many sources that the cost of developing an NCE 
could exceed $2–3 billion [9,11–13].
Toxicology is a critical part of early- and late-phase drug 
development; the role of toxicology in those processes has 
been extensively reviewed [14–18]. The purpose of toxicology 
testing programs is to characterize the toxicity of the drug can-
didate under various testing conditions (e.g., dose, treatment 
duration, route) in order to understand potentially harmful 
effects [19]. A general approach to developing a toxicity proﬁle 
for a pharmaceutical agent is given in Figure 8.2.
Regulatory writing is a critical non-laboratory activity in a 
successful approach to drug development. The importance of 
nonclinical regulatory writing, integrating discovery toxicol-
ogy, good laboratory practice (GLP) toxicology and clinical 
studies, and the impact on the drug development cycle time has 
been reviewed [20]. The number of documents produced, from 
the nonclinical summaries, the clinical investigator brochure 
through full regulatory reports, and the overall experience 
of the regulatory writer can have a large impact on the drug

development cycle time, or at least on the number of activities 
on the critical path.
During early drug discovery, the input of the toxicologist is 
often solicited in order to understand the inherent risk associ-
ated with a particular pharmacologic target. Understanding the 
likely on-target (associated with the intended pharmacology) 
risks through literature reviews, target distribution, and geneti-
cally modified models (e.g., knockout mouse models) enables 
a tailored safety assessment early in the process to define an 
appropriate margin of safety (MOS), which is typically viewed 
as the ratio of the highest dose associated with no toxicity or 
manageable toxicity to the efficacious dose in the appropri-
ate test system. In this construct, the highest dose associated 
with no toxicity is the no-observed-effect level (NOEL); the 
highest dose associated with manageable toxicity is the no-
observed-adverse-effect level (NOAEL). Off-target toxicity, 
which is associated with responses other than the intended 
pharmacologic target and inherently driven by the chemical 
structures being evaluated, is often the focus of predictive tox-
icology tools applied early in order to influence the selection 
of a chemical series, with understood or manageable risk, to 
move into lead optimization (LO).
During the early discovery process of identification of 
a lead chemical series, toxicologists employ rapid, quantita-
tive in silico and in vitro screening methods, to help identify 
potential chemical scaffolds worthy of exploring in the hopes 
of selecting a drug candidate with the best efficacy/safety 
proﬁle (Figure 8.3) [21]. Computational toxicology tools, even 
though they have limitations, are advantageous in that quan-
titative structure-activity relationships can be assessed with-
out the investment of chemical synthesis. Many of these tools 
are currently commercially available to predict toxicological 
responses (e.g., cytotoxicity, cardiovascular, and genotoxicity 
risk) and this continues to be an area of rapid growth [22,23]. 
In vitro screening methods, often using high-throughput, 
medium-throughput, or high-content approaches, allow a 
great number of compounds to be evaluated, with minimal 
amounts of material, to rank-order compounds for further 
efficacy and safety investigation [24]. Numerous cell-based 
models are used to evaluate common toxicities encountered 
in vivo (e.g., cytotoxicity, phospholipidosis, cardiovascular risk 
via human Ether-a-go-go-Related Gene (hERG) blockade, and 
mutagenicity).
The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) has published a 
roadmap identifying areas that could benefit from improved 
predictability [25]. While predictive toxicology tools continue 
FIGURE 8.1  Attrition rate of new drug candidates. (Data from Chien 
[10] and DiMasi et al. [11,12].) Representative data, advances have been 
made but the overall risks in drug development remain extremely high.
FIGURE 8.2  General approach to developing a toxicity profile for pharmaceutical agents.

Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
to develop, the LO toxicology phase of drug discovery/develop-
ment continues to perform in vivo studies to assess target organs, 
determine the dose-limiting toxicity, compare the adverse events 
with those measures of efficacy seen in the early pharmacol-
ogy evaluations (i.e., in vivo pharmacology) and generate a drug 
candidate with an appropriate MOS for the intended therapeutic 
indication in humans. The strategies employed across the phar-
maceutical industry in maximizing the LO [26] and early-stage 
development [27] have been extensively reviewed. The studies 
are often single or repeat-dose studies which are short in dura-
tion (<2 weeks), use a minimal number of animals, and have 
abbreviated pathology and toxicokinetic endpoints. The integra-
tion of pharmacology end points improves the evaluation in the 
LO phase. The LO phase is iterative in that multiple compounds 
are evaluated, in both toxicology and pharmacology studies, for 
safety, efficacy, and pharmacokinetics (PK), in order to declare 
a drug candidate worthy of advancement to definitive toxicol-
ogy studies (i.e., those toxicology studies occurring after can-
didate selection, in the regulatory phases and used to support 
first human dosing; non-definitive toxicology is a general term 
used to describe those investigative studies conducted prior to 
the regulatory phase). Sasseville et al. wrote an excellent review 
on the application of predictive tools and testing paradigms for 
drug discovery [28].
These early screening procedures used in discovery toxicol-
ogy, however, are only a prelude to the required comprehensive 
safety assessments expected from the toxicologist. Regulatory 
requirements, including guidance documents and GLPs, dic-
tate many aspects of the toxicology study protocol and must 
be followed closely for all deﬁnitive (those that support human 
studies) toxicology studies [29]. The early toxicology studies, 
conducted in the discovery phase, do not require GLP compli-
ance; however, a process of good research practices (GRPs), 
ensuring quality, validation, documentation, reproducibility, 
etc. should be applied.
Prior to initiating clinical trials, physicians need to under-
stand the toxicologic proﬁle produced by the drug candidate 
in relevant animal models. Prior to the FHD, determination 
of the relevance of animal models (e.g., metabolism relative 
to humans) is limited to in vitro evaluations using human and 
animal tissue preparations. On the basis of the clinicians’ 
needs, the toxicology proﬁle of an NCE is characterized by a 
number of questions [30]:
•	 What dose/exposure produces toxic effects in 
animals?
•	 What dose/exposure does not produce toxic effects 
in animals?
•	 Were the models relevant for predicting human 
toxicity?
•	 What were the signs and duration of toxic responses?
•	 Did effects differ following single or multiple dosing?
•	 Were the toxic responses reversible?
•	 What were the target organs or systems?
•	 Was the toxicity expected for this chemical class?
•	 Are toxic metabolites or reactive intermediates 
produced?
•	 Was accommodation to the toxic effects observed?
The answers to these questions form the basis of the toxicol-
ogy proﬁle supporting initial and continued clinical trials.
The major objectives of a toxicologic evaluation change, as a 
potential new drug candidate moves through the various stages 
of the discovery/development process were outlined by Zbinden 
[31] and are modified based on the needs of the development 
program. The relationships of the studies used to develop a toxi-
cology proﬁle, as defined in Figure 8.2, to clinical trial phases 
are shown in Figure 8.3. The early stages focus on toxicologic 
FIGURE 8.3  Representative duration of toxicology involvement in drug discovery and development showing the major milestones and study types 
by phase. The goal is to reduce development time while maintaining a focus on nonclinical and clinical safety assessments. CE, Candidate evaluation; 
CS, Candidate selection; FHD, First human dose; LI, Lead identification; LO, Lead optimization; PD, Product decision.

Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
screening (Table 8.2). A sample flow scheme from early discov-
ery toxicology (Figure 8.4) shows the stages of investigation and 
the integration of in vitro, ex vivo and in vivo safety and efficacy. 
Deﬁnitive toxicology studies are very time consuming and costly; 
thus, relatively inexpensive, short-term screening procedures are 
used to eliminate the most toxic compounds, early in the process 
[32]. At this stage, the principle of managing the toxicity based on 
target, clinical population, etc. may not be the primary decision 
driver; potentially useful drugs may be eliminated at this stage 
while looking for related molecules not expressing the toxicity.
Inherent to these initial approaches to evaluate potential 
drug toxicities are a number of imperfections: the target sys-
tems may not be routinely evaluated; the assay procedures may 
be inadequate or improperly timed relative to the onset of the 
response of interest; target-organ exposure may not be evalu-
ated; identification and quantification of adverse events may 
be inadequate; translation of effects between species may be 
inadequate; and the test model may not be an appropriate sur-
rogate for human response [33].
There is no simple answer to the often-asked question: 
“What toxicity proﬁle would cause a company to stop devel-
opment of a new drug candidate?” [34]. However, the demon-
strated toxicity of other compounds in the class, if available, 
and the gravity of the disease state under study often provide 
guidance as to what might be an acceptable safety proﬁle for 
a potential therapeutic agent. An overview representation of 
nonclinical testing strategies, in relation to clinical trial phase, 
is provided in the Pharmaceutical R&D Compendium [35]. 
This overview is managed in a case-by-case fashion, criti-
cal health issues can change the relationship as long as safety 
receives adequate attention.
In addition to the drug substance, the delivery system may 
also require nonclinical evaluation because it may alter phar-
macodynamic (action of the drug on the body) and pharma-
cokinetic (action of the body on the drug) relationships. The 
regulatory requirements for the safety assessment of known 
and novel drug delivery systems have been reviewed by 
Weissinger [36,37].

Commercially advantageous forms of genetic manipulation 
date back to antiquity (e.g., inbreeding, cross fertilization). The 
introduction of modern recombinant DNA (rDNA) technol-
ogy has had a major impact on life science research and has 
allowed for the large-scale production of protein pharmaco-
logic agents that would have been very difﬁcult to produce by 
normal chemical synthetic means [38].
A new biological entity (NBE) is deﬁned as a complex, 
high-molecular-weight material that cannot be fully charac-
terized by standard chemical analysis and which may require 
immunologic, biochemical, or bioassay techniques to measure 
the quantity present and to assess activity [39]. The develop-
ment, utility, and relative safety of human insulin [40], human 
growth hormone [41], and interferon (IFN) [42] have helped 
fuel the interest in the production of biologically active pep-
tides. As the interests of pharmacologists in biotechnology 
research expand, the difﬁculties of producing a comprehen-
sive set of safety guidelines increase. Legal deﬁnitions of bio-
technology products and regulatory guidance for nonclinical 
assessments have been reviewed by Tsang and Beers [43].
When NBEs were introduced, beginning with insulin, reg-
ulatory concepts/guidance were not in place to address the 
questions forthcoming from this new technology [44,45]. The 
regulatory questions relating to recombinant products are for-
midable, and the possibility that each biotechnology product 
might require customized safety testing has been given serious 
consideration [46,47,48]. Clearly, the immunologic response to 
foreign proteins may compromise the utility of using tradi-
tional animal models in the safety assessment of these agents. 
Other major regulatory issues include the assurance that 
recombinant production methods do not result in addition of 
contaminants and the demonstration to regulators that biosyn-
thetic products are identical to natural substances [49]. In the 
biosynthetic human insulin (BHI) approval process, meetings 
between regulatory agency and industry scientists to review 
the manufacturing process, molecular biology, and puriﬁcation 
of the hormone, as well as clinical trial programs, were criti-
cal in facilitating eventual approvals. Industry and regulatory 
agency representatives agreed that the chemistry of an NBE 
should prove its identity [50]. The identity and purity of rDNA 
insulin, therefore, received much attention [44]. Anticipation 
of problems and the communication of concerns were key 
to the rapid New Drug Application (NDA) approval for BHI 
(5.5 months). The U.S. FDA has strongly recommended that 
it be involved early in the nonclinical and clinical develop-
ment plan to facilitate the approval process for both NCEs and 
NBEs [45].
The U.S. biotechnology policy stated, 
the same physical and biological laws govern the 
response of organisms modiﬁed by modern molecu-
lar and cellular methods and those produced by clas-
sical methods … no conceptual distinction exists 
between genetic modiﬁcation of plants and micro-
organisms by classical methods or by molecular 
techniques that modify DNA and transfer genes [51]. 
Thus, it would not be expected that NBEs per se pose an 
unusual risk to human health and the environment [51]. The 
toxicologist should be aware, however, that compounds made 
via rDNA techniques are not necessarily identical to the natu-
ral material, as might be assumed [52]. Dayan [53] suggested 
that the toxicology proﬁle for an NBE should be deﬁned in 
terms of chemical identity of the material, extent of prior 
knowledge, and intended use. The U.S. Pharmaceutical 
Research and Manufacturers Association has recommended 
that nonclinical toxicologic evaluations of NBEs should 
be decided on a case-by-case basis [48], and regulatory and 
industry representatives attending the ﬁrst International 
Conference on Harmonisation of Technical Requirements for 
Registration of Pharmaceuticals for Human Use (ICH) have 
also supported this position; the current guidance supporting 
this position [54] will be discussed later. The established toxi-
cology information will guide the clinical trial and address 
possible hazards in the workplace, where humans are exposed 
to the compound, its precursors and contaminants that are also 
contained in the bulk material to be tested during the chemical 
or biological synthetic process.
As a matter of history, the ICH was conceived in 1990, at a 
meeting in Brussels Belgium; the ICH had modified its name, 
in 2015 to The International Council for Harmonisation of 
Technical Requirements for Pharmaceuticals for Human Use 
(ICH), and has expanded its membership beyond the founder 
organizations.
The toxicologist has more degrees of freedom, requiring a 
more innovative approach in designing studies for NBEs than 
when dealing with traditional NCEs [39]. As is the case with 
NCEs, however, the principal goals of the toxicologic evalu-
ation of recombinant products are to detect major toxicity, 
to identify lesser toxicity, to determine the dose relationship 
of toxic effects and their duration to guide the clinical dose 
schedule, and to investigate the mechanisms of action related 
to the toxic response. Cavagnaro [55] has provided a compre-
hensive overview of the challenges and approaches to nonclin-
ical safety evaluations of NBEs.
The three main areas of concern relative to NBEs are toxic-
ity per se, exaggerated pharmacodynamic effects (anticipated 
toxicity based on the pharmacologic mechanism of action), 
and allergic reactions (Figure 8.5) [56]. Intrinsic toxicity 
has been deﬁned as undesirable effects having no obvious 
relationship to the molecule’s pharmacodynamic properties. 
Pharmacodynamic toxicity is deﬁned as an exaggerated phar-
macologic response (i.e., hypoglycemic shock from insulin). 
Immunotoxicity has been related to hypersensitivity, cell 
transformation, and the production of neutralizing antibodies. 
The loss of the biological activity of a recombinant therapeutic 
agent through production of neutralizing antibodies and the 
development of immune-complex disease in experimental ani-
mals are factors that must be given individual attention in the 
overall safety assessment process [48]. It has been suggested 
that animal models of immunotoxicity are of limited useful-
ness in that no animal model may be fully suitable for predict-
ing the toxicity of highly species-speciﬁc proteins. Friedmann 
[57], however, has indicated that the lack of hypersensitiv-
ity reactions in response to small peptides in animal experi-
ments may be viewed as an indication of their acceptability in 
humans. Graham [58] emphasized the use of a case-by-case 
approach to toxicologic evaluations of NBEs based on their 
similarity to natural human proteins, immune response in

337
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
animal models, and production of neutralizing antibodies in 
nonclinical and clinical studies.
The unique regulatory approval of recombinant insulin most 
likely resulted in unrealistic expectations in the biotechnology 
industry regarding the rapidity of review of NBE applica-
tions [59]. Two factors will facilitate the regulatory approval 
of NBEs, as is true in the development requirements for all 
NCEs. The ﬁrst factor is therapeutic importance, and the 
FDA has established a “fast track” rapid-approval procedure 
for NBEs that target unsatisﬁed indications. The second fac-
tor is the relationship of the NBE to an established drug. It 
appears likely that new therapeutic agents derived from bio-
technology will have to satisfy all the traditional expectations 
of regulatory agencies [59]. The possibility that subtle changes 
in chemical structure may exist and may therefore inﬂuence 
PK, pharmacodynamics, or immunogenicity is used to support 
this regulatory position [60].
Questions of safety are not only properly asked about the 
NBE per se but also about contaminants or residues resulting 
from the manufacturing or puriﬁcation processes, antigenic 
variation, or reversion to the wild type of a living organism 
[39]. Worker exposure in the production process may be of 
concern due to relatively high-level, long-term exposure to var-
ious end-products of the biotechnology process (i.e., live and 
dead microorganisms and mammalian cells and their deriva-
tives) [52]. This leads to the area where traditional scientiﬁc 
approaches and techniques do not provide a satisfactory toxi-
cologic proﬁle (e.g., transfer of an immortalization factor from 
a mammalian cell, allergic reactions) [52].
The introduction of new methods/technologies is looked 
at in a positive way by most drug development scientists. 
However, as we have learned with new technologies, every 
new technology seems “better” the less we know about it. As 
we develop a more complete understanding of the true poten-
tial contributions of a new technology, over the long term, we 
realize that the promises of generating meaningful data in the 
short term were generally overstated. An example of this is the 
expectation that the Ames in vitro genetic toxicity assay would 
replace the two-year carcinogenicity assays.
New focus also takes us in interesting directions; for exam-
ple, the use of biomarkers (essentially a measured observation 
or outcome of any biological evaluation) is an old concept 
with renewed interest. The development of new procedures to 
track changes, longitudinally, is of increasing importance in 
drug discovery/development. If we think broadly enough, the 
common quote, “The dose alone makes a thing not a poison” 
[61], would have little meaning if Paracelsus (1493–1541) 
were not thinking about what are today termed “biomark-
ers”. Toxicology studies have always depended on the evalu-
ation of biomarkers, which have traditionally been used to 
conﬁrm exposure, to monitor susceptibility to a toxic agent, 
and to assess adverse effects [62]. New biomarkers continue 
to be evaluated and validated for use in both drug discovery 
and development as premonitory tools of toxicity as well as for 
possible translation to clinical trials [23].
8.2  Relevance of Animal Models in 
Toxicologic Assessment
The suitability of experimental animal data for assessing risk 
to humans as well as animal welfare concerns is important 
contemporary issues in toxicology. Animals and humans have 
much in common anatomically, physiologically, and biochem-
ically [63]. The two main guiding principles of experimental 
toxicology are that effects produced in animals, when prop-
erly qualiﬁed, are applicable to humans and that exposure 
of experimental animals to high doses of a test compound is 
necessary and valid in determining human risk [50]. Although 
it is generally agreed that animal assays are not 100% predic-
tive of human effects, they are more predictive than generally 
thought [64–66]. While the predictability of in vivo studies is 
an area of disagreement between toxicologists, historically, it 
has been reported that animal assays have been predictive of 
human toxicity in all but 10% of comparisons [67]. It must 
be recognized, however, that major differences in response 
to chemical agents can exist both within and between species 
[68]. The most serious differences between laboratory animal 
studies and human clinical trials are related to biochemical 
and physiological species differences, such as metabolism and 
genetics (hypersensitivity responses), as well as differences in 
experimental design, including quantity, route, and duration of 
drug administration [69]. It is possible that humans can be as 
much as 50 times more sensitive on a milligram-per-kilogram 
basis than experimental animals [69]. The toxicologist must 
use the appropriate allometric scaling [70] and critically eval-
uate the relevance of the chosen animal model in each safety 
evaluation.
Regulatory agencies and research-based pharmaceutical 
companies consider laboratory animal toxicology studies as a 
critical part of the assessment of new drug candidates [71,72]. 
FIGURE 8.5  Main areas of concern with response to biotechnology products. (Data from Zbinden [33,56].)

338
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Conﬁdence in the validity of experimental toxicology is based 
on the large inventory of chemically induced lesions that occur 
both in animals and humans. It may be incorrect to assume 
that what is demonstrated in animal toxicology studies will 
occur in human clinical trials, but until it is shown that the 
toxicity expressed is not relevant to humans, that assumption 
appears valid [73]. Also, until our knowledge base expands, 
animal data must be extrapolated to the human situation using 
a conservative approach (e.g., use of relatively high doses, 
assuming that humans are more sensitive than the most rel-
evant species) [74].
The ultimate goals of the toxicology assessments are to 
characterize toxicity in animal models, identify potential 
problems in short- and long-term clinical studies, identify 
the circumstances under which toxicity occurs, evaluate the 
extent to which the data warrant extrapolation to humans, rec-
ommend safe starting dose [70] and levels of exposure, and, 
finally, contribute to the decision to test the potential new 
drug candidate in humans [73,75–78]. It has been recognized 
that qualitative extrapolation of drug toxicity from animals to 
humans is more reliable than estimation of the magnitude of 
dose producing a similar effect in animals and humans; that is, 
the pharmacodynamics of an agent are more translatable than 
its PK [79]. Complicating the ability to extrapolate data from 
animals to humans are the large doses sometimes used, and 
often required, in animal studies. As a result, adverse effects 
are described that may be the result of frank intoxication and/
or overloaded mechanisms in animals with questionable rel-
evance in humans. Zbinden [80] has proposed that the ability 
of animal toxicity studies to predict potential human toxicity 
is related to the mechanism of drug action. Within limitations, 
animals and humans respond in ways similar enough, from 
a pharmacodynamic perspective, for animal toxicity evalua-
tions to serve as useful predictors of human toxicity [76,81–
84]. However, toxicologic evaluations in animals can predict 
toxic responses in humans only if the response is not unique to 
humans [71]. Those compounds that are toxic to humans but 
“relatively” nontoxic to specific animal species (i.e., rat, tha-
lidomide) are of greatest concern. The extrapolation of animal 
data to humans is likely to become even more complicated as 
molecular biology techniques continue to allow more sophis-
ticated characterization of speciﬁc human therapeutic targets 
(human enzymes, receptors) and the ultimate development of 
drugs speciﬁc for these targets continues to increase.
Table 8.3 lists common undesirable drug effects seen 
in human studies; 76% of the ﬁndings are predictable from 
animal studies. Predictability is enhanced for those adverse 
effects that can be directly related to the pharmacologic mech-
anism of action of the compound. Adverse responses com-
monly referred to as dose- and time-related are relatively well 
predicted from animal studies. It is more difﬁcult to extrapo-
late effects that are not dose- or time-related [85].
There is a small element of toxicity that cannot be observed 
until large-scale clinical studies are conducted [82]. This 
may be the result of a very low incidence of occurrence or 
idiosyncratic responses in a small subset of the patient popu-
lation; however, considering the increased use of pharmaceuti-
cal agents and the relative infrequency of major incidence of 
human toxicity, the initial laboratory studies are clearly serv-
ing a valuable function [73]. A large majority of human drug 
exposures are relatively free of unmanageable effects and in 
good concordance with the results of animal toxicity studies, 
as was first reported in 1980 [86]. The use of adequate test sys-
tems is critical to the predictive (translational) ability of ani-
mal toxicity evaluations. Cahn [87] reported that the cardiac 
effects of calcium antagonists (i.e., ectopic beats, ventricular 
tachycardia, and ventricular ﬁbrillation) were seen in humans 
but were not described in long-term animal studies. These 
effects, however, were demonstrable in animals using appro-
priate functional evaluations not always included in routine 
toxicologic testing, and it is these kinds of clinical ﬁndings 
that have contributed to the evolution of the science of regu-
latory toxicology. Oftentimes, toxicologically important end-
points, such as cardiac, pulmonary, or renal function, are not 
taken into consideration in the design of “routine” toxicology 
studies, which are frequently focused on changes in clinical 
signs and pathology. The toxicologist should be challenged 
to consider potential adverse effects related to the pharmaco-
dynamics of the test compound in the design of appropriate 
safety studies [84]. Furthermore, many new drug candidates

Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
are being targeted toward the aging population, where com-
promised hepatic or renal function may signiﬁcantly modify 
the PK and thus toxicity of the compound [88]. These examples 
emphasize the importance of using an adequate and appropri-
ate test system to evaluate the toxicity of new drug candidates.
Animal testing for biotechnology products is limited to the 
species showing relevant pharmacodynamic response as per 
humans, without showing signs of immunity [39,54,60]. This 
is only feasible when proteins are highly conserved across 
species. The production of neutralizing antibodies will limit 
the study duration and thus the support for clinical trials. 
Administration of a highly speciﬁc human protein to labo-
ratory animals for a sufﬁcient duration to produce immune-
complex disease will do little to reveal effects anticipated in 
clinical trials. Antigenicity of the test material can be a major 
complicating factor, in that the potential allergic etiology of 
all lesions developing in animals treated with human proteins 
must be considered [89]. Alternatively, nonclinical toxicology 
studies of biotechnology products may be less predictive of 
allergic responses that may occur in humans following chronic 
therapy [52]. The appropriate laboratory species for biotechnol-
ogy product testing should demonstrate similar pharmacody-
namics and adverse responses relative to humans. If an animal 
model demonstrating a similar pharmacologic response to 
humans cannot be selected, species selection based on toxic-
ity likely to be representative of that expected in humans may 
be acceptable [36]. The FDA does not currently require the 
study of recombinant proteins exclusively in primate models, 
a study in a relevant species, however, is expected. Nonhuman 
primate models demonstrate many similarities to humans at 
the molecular level and often turn out to be the most appropri-
ate species for toxicity testing of NBEs [58].
The disagreement concerning the predictability of animal 
models has become a consistent part of the drug development 
paradigm, affecting decisions on moving potential drug candi-
dates through the clinical trial phases. Animal models provide 
predictability [66], but that statement has many caveats. The 
predictability debate has as many political overtones as it does 
valid scientific questions. A few selected references will be 
used here to frame the discussion at a high level. Concordance 
of animal safety and clinical data has been reported to be 
unpredictable, and the reliability of evidence in the litera-
ture is insufficient to drive an assessment of animal to human 
concordance [90]. An umbrella study of the concordance of 
toxicity data between animals and humans was initiated by 
International Life Sciences Institute (ILSI) and reported by 
Olson et al. [91]. Overall that study found a positive concor-
dance rate with human toxicity of 71%, for two specie toxi-
cology assessments; nonrodents being 63% predictive and 
rodents alone being 43% predictive. Monticello reported on a 
pharmaceutical industry consortium database to determine the 
accuracy of assessments of concordance in human and animal 
translation of toxicology findings [92]. The initial report on 
this effort [93] based on positive predictive value, defined as 
the proportion of positive nonclinical findings that had positive 
clinical findings, and negative predictive value, defined as the 
proportion of negative nonclinical findings that had negative 
clinical findings, supported the current environment of in vivo 
testing in support of entry to clinical trials [93]; an absence of 
toxicity in animal studies strongly predicts the clinical out-
come. Avila et al. [66] have provided an FDA/Center for Drug 
Evaluation and Research (CDER) perspective on nonclinical 
testing strategies, both classical and new methodologies. They 
address the utility of the current strategies, and the state of 
development of new technologies. This will not be reviewed in 
detail here. However, some of the interesting points made will 
be briefly mentioned. The perspective acknowledges that tra-
ditional nonclinical testing methods can predict clinical out-
comes. They address the point that while the current strategies 
work, they could be better; an integration of existing and new 
approaches may be the best road forward. The areas in which 
the current strategies are less than optimal, and where, in the 
areas of general toxicology, Safety Pharmacology, carcinoge-
nicity, developmental and reproductive toxicology (DART), 
and special toxicity evaluations, the new approaches can add 
value, once more fully understood and validated is addressed 
[66]. To keep the matter in perspective, while animal models 
are not a perfect predictor of either efficacy or safety in the 
clinical trial phase, the clinical trial phase is not a perfect pre-
dictor of efficacy or safety in the marketing phase. A clinical 
trial study of tens of thousands of patients may not be suf-
ficiently large to show a subtle effect in the marketing of an 
approved medicine to a population of tens of millions.
The increased application of genomics may pave the way 
for an improved safety risk-benefit assessment, i.e., the right 
drug to the right patient. An extensive review of in silico, in 
vitro and in vivo models used to accelerate drug discovery and 
development has been published by Atkinson [94].
8.3  Drug Disposition and Kinetics 
in Drug Development
The relationship of administered dose to toxicologic response 
is not always a simple correlation. Traditionally, the adminis-
tered dose (mg/kg) has been the most commonly used expres-
sion to compare toxicologic responses between species. The 
value of the administered dose term as the most appropriate 
comparator with toxicologic response, however, has been 
questioned in scientiﬁc and regulatory circles. It is becoming 
increasingly well recognized that both beneﬁcial and toxic 
effects of therapeutic agents are dependent on the quantity 
of material reaching the target site [95]. This has led to in 
vivo exposure assessments, usually done by measurement of 
plasma or blood levels of the administered agent, being the 
most widely used and generally accepted approach to evaluate 
exposure. These measures also form the basis for any subse-
quent determinations of exposure multiples or safety margins.
Knowledge of the level of exposure is critical for under-
standing not only safety but also efﬁcacy. Fully elucidat-
ing this knowledge base provides the most robust estimates 
of safety multiples. In its simplest representation, the safety 
multiple can be calculated by dividing the highest exposure 
observed without the presence of an adverse effect by the 
exposure needed to elicit efﬁcacy. In practice, much more 
complexity is encountered in making this estimate. Variables 
such as exposure comparisons across species, the absence of

340
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
exposure determinations at the target site of activity or toxic-
ity, and nonlinearity of the dose/exposure–response compli-
cate the assessment.
Measures of exposure are also useful in establishing non-
linearity in kinetics, which is important in explaining toxic 
responses seen in particular species [95,96]. It seems more ratio-
nal to establish an upper dose in toxicology studies based on the 
linearity of kinetics rather than at the maximum tolerated dose 
(MTD), as it is often the case that the MTD falls in the range of 
nonlinear kinetics, saturating normal metabolic processes [96]. 
Thus, an animal treated at the MTD may be exposed to much 
higher levels of parent drug or toxic metabolites than would be 
observed at meaningful multiples of the clinical dose.
The relationship of administered dose to delivered dose 
remains a central issue in the interpretation of toxicology 
data. The measurement of plasma concentrations of parent 
compound and metabolites represents a partial resolution to 
this problem. There are limitations, however, in using plasma 
concentration as a relevant measure of exposure for those com-
pounds that are tissue sequestered [97]. It has been noted that, 
although many therapeutic agents achieve tissue levels propor-
tional to plasma concentration, some continue to accumulate 
in tissue with continued dosing.
Levels of exposure of test compounds or their metabolites 
at sites of action can be assessed by measuring tissue kinetics. 
Lovastatin is an example of a compound that exerts its primary 
pharmacology and toxicology at the same site. In this case, the 
kinetics of both lovastatin and its active metabolite have been 
well characterized [98]. In the overall discussion of kinetics 
and measures of exposure, it is important to understand the 
ability to distinguish parent compound from metabolite [99]. 
Perhaps the most critical aspect of the role of the toxicologist, 
in close collaboration with the kineticist, is to complete the 
multifaceted exposure comparisons, couple this knowledge 
with that of the observed effects of the test compound, and 
render an informed opinion of the overall safety proﬁle of the 
test compound under study.
Kinetic analysis has become a standard component of the 
design, and a valuable tool in the interpretation of the non-
clinical safety proﬁle of NCEs [100,101]. Various guidance has 
been issued in recent years describing objectives and speciﬁc 
recommendations for toxicokinetic evaluation [102,103]. Well-
designed toxicokinetic studies provide insight into exposure 
(typically expressed as drug concentration per unit time or 
AUC [area-under-the-plasma concentration-time curve]) as 
well as other pharmacokinetic parameters such as clearance 
(ClT), volume of distribution (Vd), half-life (T1/2). These phar-
macokinetic parameters are often further reﬁned in speciﬁc 
studies to assess absorption, distribution, metabolism, and 
elimination (ADME).
Kinetic parameters guide the assessment of dose propor-
tionality, accumulation potential upon multiple doses, sex dif-
ferences, and species differences. It is also noteworthy to point 
out that the saturation of systemic exposure may occur after 
oral dosing as a result of absorption-related processes. When 
saturation is reached, increasing the dose does not result in 
increases of exposure. Kinetic analysis is useful in determin-
ing the dose at which saturation occurs. This may then be used 
to justify the selection of a high dose in a toxicology study.
The majority of toxicokinetic assessments are designed to 
evaluate parent drug or metabolite concentrations in plasma 
[104]. These studies are usually an integrated part of the 
design of various toxicology studies, including acute, sub-
chronic, chronic, reproductive/developmental, and carcino-
genicity studies, regardless of route of administration or test 
species. An understanding of the internal exposure associated 
with dose-limiting toxicities or premonitory signs and symp-
toms that precede dose-limiting toxicities or adverse events 
is fundamental to any toxicokinetic assessment. Such assess-
ments provide the foundation for the establishment of a rela-
tionship between dose, exposure, and adverse events [105,106]. 
The role of clearance, absorption, or distribution changes in 
the relationship aid in the prediction of accumulation, satura-
tion, or decreases in exposure observed after multiple doses.
Similarities in the qualitative proﬁles of metabolism of 
xenobiotics across rat, mouse, dog, and nonhuman primates 
have been reported for decades [107,108]. Quantitative proﬁles, 
however, are often quite dissimilar and have been studied 
extensively; consequently, recommendations [104,109] have 
been published to offer additional insight into the appropri-
ate instances in which to quantify metabolites. In addition to 
the recommendations offered, multiple reports have been pub-
lished, reviewed and/or debated [110,111]. These offer greater 
insight into various approaches to investigate and analyze the 
absolute and relative exposure(s) of metabolite to parent or 
total drug-related material. Combined, these qualitative and 
quantitative assessments of an NCE early in the development 
cycle greatly aid in the selection of the appropriate preclini-
cal species for safety assessment. This early assessment is 
often coupled with the previously mentioned ADME studies. 
Increasingly, various in vitro test systems are utilized for the 
initial assessment of some ADME properties. Speciﬁcally, the 
utilization of cellular, subcellular fractions, and tissue slices 
or segments to assess absorption and metabolism are the most 
widely employed test systems. For example, permeability 
across Caco-2 cell monolayers has been well established as a 
model for gut absorption [112].
Kinetics, either PK or toxicokinetics (TK), is the time 
course of absorption, distribution, metabolism, and excretion 
(ADME) processes impacting the resident time of a drug in 
the body; the plasma concentration-time curve (Figure 8.6) is a 
graphical translation of these concerted processes. While there 
are differences in the interpretation, subtle sometimes, of PK 
and TK, these parameters will be addressed here, generally, 
as kinetics. With the advent of contemporary in vitro and in 
vivo approaches to quantitatively define the ADME character-
istics of compounds [113], toxicologists have at their disposal 
a volume of kinetic data to adequately predict the disposition 
of a NCE across multiple species during the drug discovery 
stage (Figure 8.7) and prior to initiation of the regulated safety 
assessment stage.
In particular, the kinetic parameters such as systemic drug 
clearance (CLs) and volume of distribution (Vd) are important 
inputs for the modeling of systemic exposure in the nonclinical 
species in which they were obtained. Moreover, the generation 
of kinetic data in rodents and nonrodents enables the predic-
tion of human half-life (T1/2) through allometric scaling of CLs 
and Vd [114–116] (Figure 8.8).

341
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
In terms of dose projections for toxicology assessments, 
discovery-staged kinetic data represent valuable guides for the 
selection of dose range and frequency for the repeat-dose toler-
ability assessments (rodent and nonrodent) commonly executed 
prior to nonclinical development. Specifically, the physical 
and enzymatic processes representing barriers to oral absorp-
tion as well as intestinal and hepatic metabolism impact the 
bioavailability and systemic clearance of the NCE and there-
fore are important determinants that influence the dose range 
(mg/kg) and frequency of administration selected for an NCE 
to be assessed in a multiple-dose safety assessment [113,117]. 
Early access to kinetic and exposure data from efficacy stud-
ies informs the toxicologist of potential issues related to drug 
absorption and/or drug clearance. Common issues observed 
during repeat-dose escalation studies are often related to solu-
bility-limited absorption, dissolution-rate-limited absorption, 
FIGURE 8.7  Representative drug discovery and development continuum; proposed timeline for DMPK assay and study by discovery and develop-
ment stage. (Adapted from [113,405].)
FIGURE 8.6  Illustration of the plasma concentration-time profile following an oral dose of a test article: Cmax, maximal plasma concentration; Tmax, 
time to achieve Cmax; AUC, area-under-the-plasma concentration-time curve.
FIGURE 8.8  Illustration of the prediction of human clearance through 
allometric scaling. The similarities in the relationship between anatomy 
(e.g., body weight) and physiologic processes (e.g., metabolic or renal 
clearance) across species are the basis of allometry.

342
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
active intestinal efflux (e.g., P-glycoprotein) [118] or nonlinear 
kinetics, all of which may negatively impact the ability to dose 
escalate in animals and achieve acceptable dose-proportional 
increases in systemic exposure to adequately assess the dose 
responsive safety of an NCE.
As a member of the integrated drug discovery and devel-
opment team, the toxicologist has access to early assessments 
of a drug platform or a specific compound’s drug metabolism 
and kinetics (DMPK) characteristics, including those issues 
that can impact exposure in nonclinical species or in the clinic. 
Specifically, utilization of hepatic subcellular fractions and 
hepatocytes from multiple species to define the in vitro intrin-
sic clearance and a projection of T1/2 for an NCE within multi-
ple species, typically rat, dog and nonhuman primate, provides 
the initial gauge to the identification of appropriate toxicology 
species [113]. Similarly, native or engineered, immortalized in 
vitro cell lines (e.g., Caco-2 and Madin-Darby canine kidney 
cells (MDCK), respectively) are employed to determine the 
susceptibility of an NCE (or drug platform) to active efflux (or 
uptake) mechanisms that can restrict intestinal permeability, 
limit organ access (e.g., brain, solid tumor), or facilitate biliary 
excretion or renal secretion [118]. Perhaps the most important 
consideration of the impact of metabolism and membrane per-
meability is with regards to limited oral bioavailability and 
access to the systemic circulation potentially impacting the 
ability to assess safety across a broad dose range.
The DMPK or toxicology investigator is not limited to in 
vitro assay methodologies in defining the ADME attributes or 
liabilities of a chemical series. Specific rodent and nonrodent 
study designs are commonly employed to define absolute bio-
availability or contributions of biliary (quantitation of NCE 
in bile) and/or renal excretion (quantitation of NCE in urine) 
in the disposition and clearance of an NCE. Indeed, kinetic 
assessment in rodents via parenteral administration (intrave-
nous/iv, subcutaneous/SC, intraperitoneal) is routine within 
industrial DMPK operations seeking to identify drug platform 
liabilities [113], such as high systemic clearance (contributing 
to insufficient T1/2) or nonlinear kinetics. In fact, the DMPK 
investigator will often employ a cassette kinetic screening 
approach whereby several compounds (four to five) are dosed 
simultaneously to one rat (n-in-1 rat) [119,120] in order to 
generate CLs, Vd and T1/2 and subsequently rank-order com-
pounds according to kinetic attributes. The in vivo CLs data 
is instrumental in establishing an in vitro-to-in vivo correla-
tion (IVIVC, with respect to intrinsic clearance) in a chemical 
series prior to LO (Figure 8.7). Several species including rats, 
guinea pigs, dogs, pigs and nonhuman primates are often used 
during LO to define kinetic parameters (CL and Vd) and enable 
the allometric projection of T1/2 in human [121] (Figure 8.8; 
scaling of CL).
The availability of in vitro and in vivo data sets from mul-
tiple species allows the toxicologist to:
•	 gauge potential issues related to the dose escalation 
of an NCE in an acute or chronic study
•	 identify an issue with saturable hepatic metabolism 
or active cellular transport (e.g., biliary efflux or 
renal secretion)
•	 confirm that the range of metabolites observed in 
vitro are produced in vivo in nonclinical rodent and 
nonrodent species.
While these approaches may vary between pharmaceutical 
organizations, across therapeutic areas or within drug classes, 
the following discovery timeline provides a summary of gen-
eralized in vitro and in vivo deliverables that are expected at 
the time of declaring the Drug Candidate Selection or prior 
to filing the Investigational New Drug (IND) application (see 
also Figure 8.7):
•	 Hit-to-Lead (multiple chemical series, 100s of 
compounds)
•	 Define the in vitro disposition characteristics for 
series (e.g., screening plasma protein binding 
(PPB) and metabolic stability)
•	 Establish an IVIVC on clearance and t1/2 and 
validate in vitro screening tools (e.g., micro-
somes, S9 fractions or hepatocytes)
•	 Identify PK issues with a chemical series (e.g., 
short t1/2, low F)
•	 Define systemic or tissue exposure in pharma-
cology models
•	 LO (a Lead series, 25–50 compounds)
•	 In vivo PK optimization of lead series in rodent 
and nonrodent species
•	 Identify development-limiting drug-drug inter-
actions (DDIs) of lead series
•	 Identify the contribution of drug transporters in 
tissue distribution/restriction
•	 Establish a rudimentary exposure-effect rela-
tionship (PK-PD) in pharmacology models
•	 Late LO/Drug Candidate Selection (DCS; three 
compounds)
•	 Comprehensive in vitro and in vivo ADME pro-
filing in rodent and nonrodent
•	 Support nonrodent toxicology species selection 
(e.g., dog, nonhuman primate) through PK/expo-
sure assessment and metabolite identification/
profiling comparison to human (in vitro)
•	 Prediction [122] of human T1/2 and projection of 
starting (or maintenance) clinical dose
•	 Identification of clinically relevant DDIs at 
anticipated clinical exposure(s)
•	 Establish exposure-based safety margins (TK) 
from the single and repeat-dose tolerability 
safety studies
The collective ADME and PK knowledge gained from drug 
discovery investigations are equally important for guiding the 
toxicologist toward the selection of blood, plasma or serum 
sampling time intervals for the generation of acceptable toxi-
cokinetics (TK) [117]. The term “toxicokinetics” refers to the 
PK obtained from any number of nonregulated or regulated 
studies in order to appropriately frame toxicological findings 
in relation to animal exposure. At its most basic function,

343
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
kinetic data reveal the dose-dependency in exposure as well 
as predict the durability of that exposure in repeat-dose study 
paradigms.
A properly designed study protocol produces TK data that:
•	 serves as proxy of NCE access to the biophase (i.e., 
engagement of pharmacologic targets),
•	 represents a sentinel for potential development-lim-
iting issues related to a poor absorption profile or an 
adaptive response that limits exposure upon repeat 
dosing.
In relation to both NCE and relevant metabolite exposure, the 
concept that PK and toxicokinetics represent the universal sur-
rogate of efficacy and toxicity, respectively, has transitioned 
the dose-dependent toxicity discussion to one of a concen-
tration-dependent toxicity (and efficacy). Most importantly, 
contributions from kinetic analysis provide for the generation 
of clinically relevant safety margins (e.g., ratio of NOAEL 
exposure to the anticipated clinical exposure) as well as guides 
toxicologists in the selection of species and dose frequency for 
an ensuing nonclinical toxicology study, including but not lim-
ited to the acute, chronic, reproductive and developmental, and 
carcinogenicity regulated safety assessments. The ICH has 
published guidance in this arena, specifically ICH Topic S3A 
[102] outlines the objectives of the toxicokinetic assessment 
and recommendations for the proper design and inclusion of 
the kinetics component within a nonregulated and regulated 
toxicology study. Similarly, ICH M3 outlines the importance 
of monitoring for metabolites in the multiple species TK anal-
ysis [123].
A renaissance in bioanalytical sciences [124] has facilitated 
the industry’s ability to quantify compound in blood and tis-
sue kinetic samples as well as in aqueous milieus generated 
from the aforementioned in vitro DMPK assays. In terms of 
generating basic exposures commensurate with the NOAEL or 
NOEL designations, contemporary bioanalysis has extended 
the dynamic range of small molecule and larger molecule 
(e.g., oligonucleotides and peptides) quantitation with state-
of-the-art platforms that capitalize on triple quadrupole and 
ion-trap mass spectrometry (MS) technology. Once reserved 
for kinetic analysis methods involving arduous tissue homog-
enization, ex vivo compound (and metabolite) derivatization 
and subsequent gas-chromatography-MS analysis, the arrival 
of electrospray ionization coupled liquid chromatography-
tandem MS (ESI-LC/MS/MS) [125] to the industrial DMPK 
laboratory has revolutionized the direct quantitation of drug 
in biological media with minimal work-up, greater operational 
throughput, and a significant reduction in the lower limits-of-
quantitation (LOQ). Importantly, the arrival of ESI-LC/MS/
MS also permitted the direct query of the bloodstream and 
excreta for the presence of drug metabolites (e.g., hydroxyl-
ated metabolites) and drug conjugates (e.g., glucuronides). 
The ability to rapidly develop LC/MS methods that display 
limited sample-to-sample carryover and are amenable to 
automated sample preparation and back-end data analysis has 
advanced the throughput in the nonregulated, discovery stage 
[126]. However, the FDA has recently published a guidance 
document for the industry that outlines rigorous tenets of both 
method development and validation when transitioning to 
preclinical development; guidelines related to selectivity and 
specificity, sensitivity, accuracy, precision, recovery, and ana-
lyte stability are adequately prescribed within the published 
guidance [127].
Not surprisingly, advancements in LC/MS-based bio-
analysis and accompanied dynamic range and lower limits 
of quantification (LOQ) have coincided with an increased 
scrutiny from regulatory bodies regarding the qualification 
of metabolite safety prior to clinical trial initiation. Those 
instances where the biotransformation of a parent compound 
produces either a human unique metabolite or an anticipated 
clinically relevant metabolite(s) displaying insufficient expo-
sure in one or more toxicology species have been detailed 
previously in both FDA guidance and supplementary com-
mentaries [128,129]. Similar to discovery-staged kinetics 
evaluations, an early in vitro and in vivo survey of metabo-
lite formation in multiple species serves to qualify/select the 
nonclinical species in preparation for clinical development. 
A number of in vitro drug metabolism systems (cryopre-
served hepatocytes and hepatic subcellular fractions rich 
in metabolizing enzymes [130]) have been demonstrated as 
high-fidelity indicators of the range of oxidative (Phase I) and 
conjugative (Phase II) metabolites one can expect in rodent, 
dog, nonhuman primate and human and are valuable tools for 
the selection of clinically relevant toxicology species for the 
impending safety assessment [131,132]. The inclusion of these 
multispecies in vitro systems is a mainstay approach for the 
production and structural elucidation of metabolites as well 
as for measuring the rates of metabolism catalyzed by Phase I 
enzymes such as the microsomal expressed cytochrome P450 
(P450) and flavin monooxygenases, aldehyde oxidase (cyto-
sol) and monoamine oxidase (mitochondria); similarly, Phase 
II enzymes located within S9 fractions (S9) catalyze conju-
gation reactions (glucuronidation/UGT; sulfation/SULT) and 
often contribute directly to drug clearance. However, while 
these tools provide a projection of the range of metabolites 
formed, they fall short in providing the relative concentra-
tion of each drug metabolite one may expect in circulation 
in vivo. The importance of kinetic analysis in the elucidation 
of species and sex-dependent steady-state concentration and 
residence time of metabolites cannot be overstated; there are 
contemporary reviews, case studies, and regulatory guidance 
(ICH and FDA) outlining the deliverables of metabolite iden-
tification and quantitation at the time of the preclinical drug 
safety assessment [123,128].
Confirmation of metabolite exposure and qualification of 
the rodent and nonrodent toxicology species ultimately comes 
from comparison of nonclinical and clinical radiolabeled 
(14C or 3H) ADME studies and the FIH non-GLP metabolite 
scouting exercise during the Phase I kinetics and tolerability 
study [133,134]. The timing of a mass balance study (absolute 
quantitation of NCE/metabolites in plasma, blood, and excreta; 
concentration in mass of NCE per tissue mass, e.g., ng/g) var-
ies with company risk strategy, drug class and disease inter-
vention strategy but typically the execution of the nonclinical 
and clinical 14C/3H-drug ADME investigations commence

344
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
during late nonclinical development and the Phase II program, 
respectively (Figure 8.7) [133,134]. The availability of radio-
labeled (3H or 14C) test article is also important when assess-
ing the extent of tissue distribution of an NCE or in those 
instances where plasma kinetics do not adequately describe 
in vivo residence time or reflect NCE access to the biophase 
in the nonclinical species pharmacology model and/or toxi-
cology species. From small molecule NCEs to large molecule 
experimental therapeutics, access to a comprehensive set of 
harvested organ tissues (at necropsy) presents the toxicologist 
with a means to quantitate (via radiometric detector-coupled-
LC/MS/MS) [133,134] the distribution of NCE/metabolites 
throughout the test animal. Similarly, a whole-body autoradi-
ography (WBA) of the segmented animal (e.g., rodent or non-
human primate) presents the toxicologist with the opportunity 
for a cross-sectional quantitation of parent NCE in an animal 
(WBA-kinetic profile) [103,135].
Biophase access by means of the systemic circulation is 
a primary tenet of the drug exposure-effect relationship. 
Therefore, total body exposure is primarily achieved by LC/
MS/MS quantitation of plasma (or whole blood, serum) and 
expressed as concentration (mass per volume) or exposure as 
the area-under-the-time-concentration curve (mass × time per 
volume) [102]. Kinetic parameters such as clearance, volume 
of distribution, and half-life are of limited value during toxi-
cology studies, where high doses typically utilized often lead 
to nonlinear kinetics and disproportionate changes in drug 
exposures. Thus, as displayed in Figure 8.6 the applicable 
plasma kinetic parameters to be reported from a toxicology 
study kinetic analysis are the maximal plasma concentra-
tion (Cmax, ng/mL), the time to achieve Cmax (Tmax, hr) and the 
plasma exposure (AUC; ng*hr/mL); these parameters are 
essential to alerting the toxicologist to dose proportionality in 
exposure or supra-proportional increase in AUC upon multiple 
dosing due to saturation of drug clearance. The selection of an 
oral drug candidate that displays low clearance, with moderate 
to high aqueous solubility and permeability, one would typi-
cally observe a dose-proportional increase in AUC (Figure 8.9, 
solid line).
Alternatively, an NCE displaying high clearance (equal to 
hepatic blood flow) is likely to produce a scenario where pre-
systemic clearance (bioavailability) is saturated at high doses 
and produces a supra-proportional dose-exposure relation-
ship within the upper dose range (Figure 8.9, dashed line, and 
Figure 8.10, dotted line).
In the event where an NCE displays poor aqueous solubility 
or permeability, the toxicologist may observe a dose-exposure 
relationship characterized by a less-than-proportional plateau 
at the upper dose range (Figure 8.11); a corresponding inability 
to dose escalate in terms of Cmax is also observed (Figure 8.12) 
(60 mg (dashed line) versus 120 mg (dotted line) oral doses).
The absorption scenario described is similar to the result 
obtained from absorption rate-limited kinetics, where a hypo-
thetical set of various solid oral dosage forms of an NCE 
administered at a dose of 60 mg can produce varied absorption 
rate constants (ka), resulting in a reduction in AUC and a con-
comitant right shift in Tmax (Figure 8.13). This characteristic 
shift in Tmax and resulting plasma concentration-time profile 
is similar to what is observed with so-called sustained release 
oral formulations of small molecule therapeutics. The TK 
parameters are equally useful in recognizing sex differences 
within a dose group, or in identifying species differences in 
exposure to an NCE [102].
An inability to achieve repeat-dose-exposure proportionality 
may be an indicator of NCE- or metabolite-mediated perturba-
tion of drug-metabolizing enzyme activity or the regulation 
and expression thereof (intestinal and/or hepatic) [117,136]. For 
example, an increase in systemic exposure between the last day 
FIGURE 8.9  Illustration of the dose (mg) dependency in drug exposure (AUC); dose-proportional increase in exposure (solid line) and supra-propor-
tional increase in exposure (dashed line), the latter of which may result from a saturation in drug clearance at higher doses (nonlinearity).

345
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
of dosing compared to the initial dose may be an indication 
of the inhibition of one (or more) cytochrome P450 enzymes 
mediating the biotransformation of the NCE. Alternatively, 
a decrease in the systemic exposure observed upon multiple 
dosing (e.g., 5 days repeat-dose tolerability study or 1-month 
chronic dosing study) may be an indicator of P450 auto induc-
tion, an adaptive response that regulates drug-metabolizing 
enzyme expression as a result of steady-state NCE exposure 
(Figure 8.14) [113,136]. The multispecies ADME and kinetic 
data collected for an NCE during drug discovery are often pre-
dictive of the types of dose-exposure profiles observed upon 
ascending, repeat-dose studies executed during regulated drug 
development (Figure 8.7).
The culmination of in vitro ADME data and in vivo kinet-
ics obtained from multiple species discovery and nonclini-
cal staged toxicology studies forms the basis of a critical 
FIGURE 8.11  Incomplete or saturation of intestinal absorption may produce a plateau in the dose:exposure relationship (dashed line) following oral 
administration of a test article; compare to dose-proportional absorption (solid line).

from nonlinear PK at 120 mg (dotted line).

assumption in the ultimate kinetic analysis; that is, the com-
mensurate increase in metabolite(s) exposure with an increased 
dose of NCE. These assumptions extended with respect to the 
extent of drug metabolism, the qualification of the toxicol-
ogy species in the production of human metabolites, and the 
prediction of metabolite resident times that are reflective of 
clinical exposure are assembled with largely discovery-staged 
ADME data and accompanied by comprehensive kinetics 
[113,117]. The partnership between the DMPK scientist and 
toxicologist is necessary, and appropriately forged early in the 
pharmaceutical testing continuum and extends into the clinical 
trial period in collaboration with the regulated bioanalytical 
and clinical pharmacology team. This integrated team may be 
responsible for initiating nonclinical studies that agencies may

a decreasing absorption rate constant (ka).

Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
request of the sponsor during late clinical trials and/or during 
the post-approval (NDA), or Phase IV period of drug develop-
ment (Figure 8.7) where DMPK support may be required for 
special population kinetics and DDI studies or the risk evalua-
tion and mitigation strategy program.
8.4  Toxicology Guidelines
8.4.1  Drug Development Timelines
Development time (e.g., cycle time) is an important focus for 
the pharmaceutical industry. Historically, available data indi-
cate a development timeline of about 10–12 years, with very 
wide variability, for new medicinal products. The pharma-
ceutical industry and the FDA have committed to increas-
ing drug discovery/development efﬁciency, hoping this leads 
to a decrease in the mean drug development time, delivery 
of innovative pharmaceuticals to patients more quickly and 
realization of sufficient proﬁtability to maintain aggressive 
research and development efforts. The FDA, specifically, had 
committed to increasing the development speed of safe and 
effective drugs through the Critical Path Initiative, described 
in Innovation/Stagnation: Challenge and Opportunity on the 
Critical Path to New Medical Products [137]. This program 
was initiated in 2004, the broad impact of these efforts is still 
being discussed and evaluated. One can look at various online 
sources of information on drug development cycle time and 
find numbers indicating a range of 10–15 years, matching drug 
development cycle time from the 1980s [138,139]. Overall 
development timelines, reported to be 13.8 years, continue to 
increase, primarily due to increased clinical trial cycle times, 
primarily in Phase II and phase III [140]. As mentioned, the 
variability in estimates of drug development cycle times is 
large, there are some development programs that, for various 
reasons, move very fast, 6 years.
New biological entities generally have had a shorter devel-
opment time (approximately 6–9 years) than NCEs. The NBEs 
registered to date have generally been well-characterized nat-
ural molecules, and the shorter development time is probably 
related to a better understanding of their actions in humans. 
The introduction of analogs of natural proteins, some designed 
to be used at supraphysiologic levels, has led to an increase in 
development time for NBEs. Information on pharmaceutical 
development cycle time and productivity has been evaluated 
by the Tufts Center for the Study of Drug Development, Tufts 
University [141].
For all molecules, NCE or NBE, the development time is 
ultimately impacted by the ability to demonstrate both efficacy 
and safety in nonclinical and clinical studies.
8.4.2  Regulatory Guidelines for Toxicity Testing
In this section, the toxicology support packages for the reg-
istration of NCEs and NBEs are reviewed from slightly dif-
ferent perspectives. More detailed information on the speciﬁc 
studies conducted, their results, and their interpretation are 
included for the classical agents (omeprazole and zidovudine), 
because many compounds currently in development would fall 
into this category and thus require similar testing strategies. 
Omeprazole was selected for discussion, though a historical 
example, because it had a comprehensive toxicology package 
at the time of regulatory submission and represents an exam-
ple of where additional mechanistic studies were critical in 
the approval process. Zidovudine [azidothymidine (AZT)] is 
discussed due to its proposed use in life-threatening disease 
where no adequate therapy was available. The rapid approval 
of the drug, in spite of signiﬁcant toxicology ﬁndings and an 
abbreviated toxicology support package, demonstrated the 
inherent ﬂexibility in the approval system even with regard to 
NCEs.
FIGURE 8.14  Time-dependent change in drug clearance. Example compound (black bars) displaying time-independent drug exposure for a same 
daily dose at Day 1 and Day 7 (no change in drug clearance). Compare to second compound (gray bars) demonstrating time-dependent change in expo-
sure for same daily dose at Day 7 versus Day 1; the increase in drug clearance signifies drug-metabolizing enzyme induction.

348
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
The discussion of speciﬁc human NBEs (gonadotropin-
releasing hormone (GnRH) analogs, IFN, human insulin) is 
presented from a more philosophical perspective. Because 
these agents are naturally occurring and because the major 
limiting toxicity in animal studies (immunogenicity) is not 
applicable to clinical trials, the design of the toxicology 
package posed special issues that were considered on a case-
by-case basis. Furthermore, the toxicologic proﬁle was antici-
pated based on extensive clinical experience with less speciﬁc 
agents (e.g., animal-derived insulin) or a broad understanding 
of the physiological functions of the hormones. The following 
discussion of the NBEs poses questions, concerns, and general 
guidelines to be considered in the development of these agents.
8.4.2.1  Acute, Subchronic, and Chronic Testing
Toxicity testing programs to support drug development are 
composed of several major study types [75]. Acute (single-
dose), subchronic (multiple doses; less than 6 months’ dura-
tion), and chronic (multiple doses; greater than or equal to 6 
months’ duration) studies are intended to elucidate the target 
organs for toxicity, demonstrate dose-response relationships, 
and often are useful in determining potential mechanisms of 
toxic action. The single and repeat-dose toxicology studies 
completed in LO are often leveraged to inform both dose justi-
fication and study endpoints in the definitive toxicology studies. 
For dose selection, consideration of the MTD, the maximum 
feasible dose and limit dose based on regulatory guidance, is 
necessary to ensure the doses selected to maximize the pos-
sibility of defining the toxicological response [142].
A variety of endpoints are routinely evaluated in subchronic 
and chronic studies, including body weight, food consump-
tion, hematology, clinical chemistry, urinalysis, and gross and 
histologic pathology of numerous tissues. A list of common 
parameters assessed in subchronic and chronic studies is pre-
sented in Table 8.4; however, the toxicologist is continually 
challenged to modify the study design to address the antici-
pated actions of the compound under investigation. This may 
result in the addition of certain parameters or tissues to be 
evaluated or a more comprehensive analysis of tissues (i.e., 
electron microscopic evaluation, immunohistochemistry). 
Furthermore, previous studies, or knowledge of the toxicity 
of other agents in the therapeutic class or those that have a 
similar structure may suggest alternative assessments, such as 
the determination of the agent to induce hepatic microsomal 
enzymes, cause phospholipid accumulation, or result in per-
oxisome proliferation.
An assessment of bioavailability and PK is often an impor-
tant endpoint of subchronic and chronic studies. As discussed 
previously, these data are critical to extrapolate toxicity 
ﬁndings to humans. Often, the toxicokinetic proﬁle of the com-
pound is determined early and late in the study so the potential 
for drug accumulation can be revealed. Alternatively, drug lev-
els may be lower toward study termination or the metabolite 
proﬁle may differ due to the induction of drug-metabolizing 
enzymes. Tissues may also be collected for drug analysis, so 
levels in affected tissues can be related to the extent of the his-
topathologic ﬁndings. Finally, important dose-response rela-
tionships can be established, relative to both parent compound 
and metabolites, that may be critical in the interpretation of 
toxicity data.
Subchronic (usually 2-week or 1-month studies) testing is 
required prior to the FHD. One-month studies in one rodent 
species (usually rat or mouse) and one nonrodent species (usu-
ally dog or nonhuman primate) generally will support up to 
1 month of dosing in humans. Where possible, the animal 
studies should be carried out using the same route of adminis-
tration anticipated for use in patients. As an aside, it should be 
acknowledged that clinical studies may initially be conducted 
by the intravenous route, regardless of the desired ultimate 
route of administration, particularly for those molecules that 
are anticipated to show efﬁcacy rapidly, to demonstrate the 
proof of concept of a new pharmacologic mechanism. Drug 
developers might thus avoid the time and expense associated 
with formulation development and maximization of the desired 
properties of the chemical if it has been demonstrated that the 
molecule or mechanism is ineffective. Clinical trials approved 
to be conducted under this paradigm require the support of 
intravenous toxicology assessments. In these circumstances, 
it is extremely important to evaluate the risk associated with 
the potential for demonstrating toxicity in the intravenous 
study that may not be relevant to the ultimate clinical route of 
administration.
Based on indication, new biomarkers, clinical experience, 
etc., other parameters will be added by toxicologist and/or 
pathologist.
Phase II and III efﬁcacy testing in patients is supported by 
longer term toxicology studies. Depending on the proposed 
duration of human exposure, toxicity studies to support phases 
II and III may be of 3-, 6-, or 9/12-months’ duration. Two or 
more subchronic or chronic studies may be conducted simul-
taneously (e.g., 3-month and 6-month studies may be initiated 
at the same time) so patients can be placed on the trial earlier 
(upon completion of the 3-month study) and maintained on the 
trial longer (supported by the ongoing 6-month study) if the 
human efﬁcacy and safety data support continued therapy. A 
potential problem with this approach is that dose selection for 
the more extended study may be found to be inadequate (doses 
either too low or too high).
Much discussion had surrounded the utility of 1-year non-
clinical studies. The FDA had been a strong proponent of 
the 1-year study approach [143], but Japan and the European 
Union (EU) have suggested that 1-year studies reveal little new 
information beyond that gained from 6-month studies. These 
data have been reviewed by Lumley et al. [144], who suggested 
that, for 154 compounds for which short-term (≤6 months) and 
long-term (>6 months) animal data are available, tests last-
ing longer than 6 months (excluding carcinogenicity studies) 
have not provided new substantive safety information. They 
also pointed out that, although new ﬁndings became evident 
after 6 months of treatment in 9 out of 75 cases, the data did 
not inﬂuence the decision of whether to continue the develop-
ment of the compound. Parkinson et al. [145] have reported 
that long-term toxicity studies in dogs provide little new data 
when compared to 3-month dog studies in conjunction with 
short- and long-term rodent studies.
The ICH committees have resolved this difference of opin-
ion 146138]. We will review the ICH guidelines below, but the

349
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
reader is encouraged to connect with the ICH website: http://
ich.org, for an up-to-date review of all current ICH guidelines.
8.4.2.2  Additional Toxicology Studies 
to Support Clinical Trials
Other tests conducted prior to initial clinical trials include 
mutagenicity (genetic toxicity) studies and pharmacologic 
assessments (Safety Pharmacology, e.g., cardiovascular, cen-
tral nervous system (CNS), and/or gastrointestinal function). 
A variety of mutagenicity studies are currently employed that 
assess various types of DNA damage in vitro and in vivo in an 
attempt to predict the oncogenic potential of the compound 
under investigation. In pharmacologic screening, the ability of 
the compound to produce toxicities or “side effects” based on 
its pharmacologic mechanism of action (on-target) is assessed;
