designing an immunotoxicity protocol [260,262,263]. The 
recent FDA draft Guidance on immunotoxic potential [260] 
specifically addresses developmental and juvenile studies 
and the formation of a functional immune system. Collinge 
et al. [264] published a review of developmental immunotox-
icity, with recommendations for future approaches. As they 
evaluated triggers for immunotoxicology testing, an emphasis 
was placed on the findings in traditional toxicity evaluations, 
and additional research in translational immunotoxicology. 
Brennan et al. [265] undertook an assessment of monoclonal 
antibodies relative to immunotoxicity, specifically addressing 
the evaluation in reproductive and developmental toxicology 
and juvenile animal studies; the role of placental transfer and 
the importance of understanding the impact of specific immu-
nomodulatory drugs on the developing immune system.
Existing draft and reissued regulatory guidance incorpo-
rate many of the ideas expressed in the literature, for example, 
integration with traditional toxicity evaluations, weight-of-
evidence approaches. Therefore, it is always advisable to 
keep informed through the governmental websites addressing 
expectations for nonclinical toxicology studies (Table 8.7).
The application of nonspeciﬁc immunotherapy for bacterial 
and viral diseases has led to an increased level of importance 
in the determination of immunotoxic effects. The standard 
toxicology studies that form the basis of toxicologic evalua-
tions should be complemented with speciﬁc evaluations use-
ful in determining functional effects on the immune system, 
especially if the agent in question is a known immune modula-
tor [259]. Immune system function results from a balance of 
the activities of various cellular components and their soluble 
factors [192], and an alteration in any factor could result in an 
imbalance of the entire system [266]. The effects of immuno-
modulating agents, therefore, could result in either enhance-
ment (e.g., hypersensitivity, autoimmunity) or suppression 
(e.g., decreased host resistance) [259,266].
Several classical-tier approaches to immunotoxicity test-
ing have been proposed [258,267]; for example, the National 
Toxicology Program (NTP) has proposed an immunotoxicol-
ogy testing strategy that includes a limited number of func-
tional and host resistance assays [267]. The two-tier approach 
consists of a screen (Tier 1), which represents a limited effort 
that includes the assessment of cell-mediated immunity, 
humoral immunity, and immunopathology. Tier 1 provides 
little information on the speciﬁcity of an observed immune 
defect or its relevance to the host; however, it can detect an 
immune alteration resulting from drug exposure [258]. Tier 2 
represents an in-depth evaluation, initiated only if functional 
changes are seen in Tier 1 at otherwise nontoxic doses [258]. 
The in-depth immune function and host resistance evaluations 
provide information on the mechanisms of the immunotoxic-
ity and aid risk assessment. Luster et al. [258] have reported 
that no compound evaluated to date has been found to produce 
an effect in Tier 2 without demonstrating some effect in Tier 1. 
The NTP pathology specs for detection of immune alterations 
following chemical or drug exposure in rodents have been out-
lined in a specification document for development and vali-
dation of immune function methodology [268]. The concept 
of performing functional tests is critical to deﬁning potential 
mechanisms of the toxic response and their applicability to 
humans. An international collaborative effort has focused on 
the evaluation of limited pathology or enhanced pathology 
evaluations to better understand potential immunotoxicity 
[254]. The enhanced pathology approach (e.g., weight deter-
mination, examination of additional lymphoid organs, grading 
of changes in the principal compartments of lymphoid tissue) 
was determined to provide an advantage in revealing effects 
on the immune system. Germolec et al. [269] have provided 
insights into the development of immunotoxicology to include 
in vitro methods in the assessment of both drug and chemical 
immune function.
The direction for pharmaceutical development is to include 
tests of potential immune system involvement in the tradi-
tional toxicologic evaluations for subchronic and chronic tox-
icity. Due to the sensitivity of the immune system to toxicants 
that could adversely affect the critical balance of the various 
immune factors and the adverse health effects that could ensue, 
it is extremely important to deﬁne any potential interaction of 
a new drug with immune system function [255].
As can be anticipated from the previous discussion, immu-
nogenicity is a major scientiﬁc issue relative to the development

368
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
of biotechnology products. Concern has been raised over 
comparison of the recombinant protein and the naturally 
occurring protein, as animal models are thought to be inad-
equate to assess the chemically subtle, but potentially immu-
nologically signiﬁcant, differences in the human response 
to these molecules. It has been assumed that a recombinant 
protein, designed for human use would produce a number of 
adverse effects (e.g., the production of neutralizing antibod-
ies) in experimental animals. It has now become clear, through 
chronic exposures in nonclinical studies, that some low-molec-
ular-weight human proteins are not immunogenic in animals 
or are only weakly so. They have also been observed not to 
produce neutralizing antibodies. In the case where antibodies 
to human proteins have been detected in nonclinical studies, 
they do not necessarily cause expected immunopathology or 
neutralization activity. The rhesus monkey has been shown to 
predict the relative immunogenicity of several recombinant 
proteins in humans [270] and may serve as a good model.
A further question is “Should all recombinant DNA prod-
ucts be routinely screened in animals prior to their introduction 
into humans?” The major reason for conducting immunotox-
icity evaluations in experimental animals is to detect those 
compounds that could induce anaphylaxis or anaphylactoid 
reactions in humans [271]. New molecules, previously mini-
mally tested in animals, such as enkephalins, would have a 
greater potential risk than well-known molecules, such as 
insulin. An approach to testing recombinant proteins as well as 
NCEs for immunogenicity or antigenicity has been suggested 
based on the extent of the clinical database and the existing 
regulatory requirements (Figure 8.17). Although studies in 
animals seem well justiﬁed for poorly characterized chemi-
cals, it remains an open question whether or not regulatory 
agencies will accept an existing extensive clinical database 
as justiﬁcation for not performing immunogenicity evalua-
tions. Again, the chemical characterization of the recombinant 
product relative to the natural material will have some bearing 
on this debate.
The importance of deﬁning the potential interaction 
between a new drug and the immune system is emphasized in 
the new regulatory approaches addressing immunotoxicology. 
The FDA has issued a guidance for industry and a draft guid-
ance [260,272] that identiﬁes major areas of focus for immu-
notoxicity studies:
•	 Immunosuppression (decreased immune function)
•	 Immunogenicity (immune reaction to drugs)
•	 Hypersensitivity (immunological sensitization)
•	 Autoimmunity (immune reaction to self-antigens)
•	 Adverse immunostimulation (antigen-nonspeciﬁc 
uncontrolled activation of the immune system)
The European Agency for the Evaluation of Medicinal 
Products (EMEA) had also published a position on immuno-
toxicology [273]. The major difference between the approaches 
is the mandatory requirement for functional testing in the 
EMEA guidance [274]. The FDA guidance [260,272] supports 
a weight-of-evidence approach involving standard tests for 
each potential new drug [275]. In addition to the current and 
historical regulatory positions, histopathologic evaluation of 
lymphoid organs and tissues remains important in identifying 
potential immunotoxicity [276].
The ICH has agreed to harmonize the immunotoxicol-
ogy guidance (Table 8.6). They have published a guidance on 
a weight-of-evidence decision-making approach to address 
nonclinical testing for immunosuppression induced by low-
molecular-weight drugs; the guidance does not directly apply 
to biotechnology-derived pharmaceuticals [259]. For the pur-
pose of the guidance, immunotoxicity is defined as unintended 
immunosuppression or enhancement. Although the guidance 
does not specify how each immunotoxicity study is performed, 
FIGURE 8.17  Proposed approach for addressing immunogenicity and antigenicity issues with established and novel biotechnology products and 
NCEs. (From Wierda, D., Personal communication, Eli Lilly and Company, Greenfield, IN, 1992.)

369
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
there is a rich history of the conduct of studies to determine the 
immunotoxicity of pharmaceuticals and chemicals, and it does 
address the general approach to immunotoxicity testing for new 
and marketed drugs and provides a flow diagram for the appli-
cation of recommended immunotoxicity evaluations. The guid-
ance does make a strong connection with the use of the standard 
toxicity studies, and with the use of in vivo pharmacology stud-
ies to provide information leading to a weight-of-evidence deci-
sion. As with all approaches to drug development, for example, 
in vivo, in vitro, it is often better to integrate disciplines and 
techniques/protocols rather than create an all-or-none approach.
8.7.3  Genetic Toxicology
Genotoxicity has been defined as the ability of either a chemi-
cal or physical agent to damage DNA, resulting in a mutation 
[277]. The approach to genetic toxicology fits with an impor-
tant element of toxicology, that is, the early identification of 
potentially hazardous substances [226]. Because the actions 
of toxicants are ultimately exerted at the cellular level, iso-
lated cell systems represent an important model for identify-
ing toxic effects. In vitro assays allow a greater control over 
xenobiotic metabolism (e.g., addition of enzymes or inhibitors) 
and facilitate mechanistic studies that could not be performed 
in vivo [278]. In vitro tests generally provide a reasonable 
approximation of the potential for an agent to have an effect 
on genetic material; in vivo procedures provide a better test 
for the potential for genetic alterations to occur in the intact 
organism [279]. The short-term in vitro tests for genotoxicity, 
potentially predictive of in vivo carcinogenicity, are among 
the most important techniques available for the rapid deter-
mination of potential severe undesirable effects of compounds 
selected for development. They are also useful in the priori-
tization of compounds to be studied in the more extended 
and expensive in vivo toxicology studies. A number of assays 
are available to evaluate the potential for genotoxicity. The 
majority opinion is in favor of a battery approach to identify 
potential genotoxic activity because different assays assess 
different types of genetic damage and no one assay is capable 
of detecting all genotoxic mechanisms relevant in tumorigen-
esis [278,280]. The ICH has published an updated guideline 
on how to conduct genotoxicity tests that includes the recom-
mended standard genotoxicity testing battery for evaluations 
of pharmaceuticals [281]. The ICH test battery includes:
•	 Gene mutation in bacteria to detect relevant genetic 
changes and the majority of genotoxic rodent 
carcinogens
•	 In vitro mammalian cell chromosomal aberration, 
the in vitro micronucleus assay, or in vitro mouse 
lymphoma thymidine kinase assay to detect chromo-
somal damage
•	 In vivo assessment of chromosomal effects that 
allows evaluation of additional relevant factors (e.g., 
absorption, distribution, metabolism, excretion), 
through the analysis of micronuclei in erythrocytes 
or chromosome aberrations in metaphase cells of the 
bone marrow
This battery may be expanded or modified when appropriate, 
such as when compounds with structural alerts are negative in 
the three standard tests or replacing an in vitro mammalian 
cell assay with the well-conducted in vivo assays [281].
Excellent reviews of methods to study the genotoxic poten-
tial and issues concerning nongenotoxic, but carcinogenic 
chemicals are available [271,282–285]. In vivo exposure assays 
have the advantage of an intact metabolic system to effectively 
assay those compounds that must be activated (metabolized to 
a reactive entity) to achieve an effect. The in vitro assays may 
be conducted with or without the addition of a postmitochon-
drial supernatant fraction from livers of rats treated with poly-
chlorinated biphenyls to maximally induce drug-metabolizing 
enzyme activities and thus enhance the detection of indirect-
acting agents.
In vitro genotoxicity assays are often used in the early drug 
discovery process aimed at selecting drug candidates for fur-
ther development. Often, when positive genotoxicity results 
emerge, in silico and in vitro screens are commonly used much 
earlier in the discovery process to guide the chemistry to mol-
ecules with no genotoxic risk. The definitive in vitro genotox-
icity evaluations of mutation and chromosomal damage are 
generally submitted prior to the FHD. The complete battery 
of recommended tests should be submitted prior to Phase II 
clinical development.
Conducting genotoxicity screens on NBEs has been an area 
of much discussion. The ICH has recognized that the standard 
genotoxicity testing battery is not applicable to NBEs and gen-
erally should not be conducted [54].
8.7.4  Safety Pharmacology
Safety Pharmacology is based on the history of observations 
that organ function can be a toxicology target [286]. In the past, 
organ function was not always addressed in standard toxicol-
ogy studies; with the increased attention to biomarkers, how-
ever, that gap is more frequently filled. Safety Pharmacology 
involves establishing the pharmacologic proﬁle of new drug 
candidates by evaluating the pharmacodynamics related to the 
therapeutic indication and by evaluating the pharmacodynam-
ics on organ systems not related to the therapeutic indication 
(off-target) [286,287]. These studies are usually conducted at 
doses well below those used to establish the toxicology safety 
proﬁle. It is not necessary or even desirable to produce frank 
toxicity to establish a valid pharmacologic proﬁle. Methods 
and strategies for in vitro pharmacological profiling, allowing 
for the early detection of off-target pharmacological effects 
have been reviewed by Bowes et al. [288]. All toxicologists, 
especially those involved in the discovery approaches to drug 
development should be aware of the recommended targets pre-
sented in this review, including G protein–coupled receptors, 
ion channels, enzymes, transporters, and nuclear receptors 
[288].
By definition [289], pharmacology studies are divided into 
three categories: primary pharmacodynamics; secondary phar-
macodynamics, and safety. The traditional approach to Safety 
Pharmacology is primarily focused on the third category. 
However, the integration of these techniques with the basic 
pharmacologic assessment of the primary pharmacodynamics

370
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
(i.e., cardiovascular pharmacology) is essential in making 
good decisions about drug development. Safety Pharmacology 
issues and issue resolution have been extensively reviewed 
[290], though primarily focused on issue resolution, the 
review broadly covers approaches, species selection and 
methods. Recently, The Safety Pharmacology Society con-
ducted an industry survey examining the practices primarily 
related to cardiovascular study design and data interpretation 
[291]; they concluded that practices in cardiovascular Safety 
Pharmacology have been remarkably stable, relative to the use 
of hERG and an in vivo core battery. The survey found that 
respiratory evaluations were rarely involved in drug-induced 
functional issues in the nonclinical safety package [291]. It 
was also noted that the cardiovascular safety concerns had 
broadened beyond the risk for Torsades de Pointe. The sur-
vey reported a support for an integrated risk assessment strat-
egy, for example, comprehensive in vitro proarrhythmic assay 
(CiPA).
The inclusion of Safety Pharmacology approaches in general 
toxicology studies should be considered whenever possible; in 
this context, the impact of potentially large doses must be con-
sidered in the ability to detect the desired responses. This topic 
has been reviewed in a pharmaceutical industry survey [292]. 
The survey supported the observation that functional adverse 
effects were an important component of drug failure. The sur-
vey also indicated an increasingly common inclusion of Safety 
Pharmacology endpoints in rodent toxicology studies and a 
more frequent inclusion in non human primate (NHP) studies 
than in canine toxicology studies. The advantage pointed out 
is the opportunity to obtain early safety assessment data. The 
dose-response design of toxicology studies should provide a 
dose where the evaluation of Safety Pharmacology parameters 
is appropriate. The selection of dose within the design of a 
traditional toxicology study, including Safety Pharmacology, 
and the justification should be reviewed with an appropriate 
regulatory body before initiating the study. In today’s environ-
ment, the recommended integration of Safety Pharmacology 
evaluations in the design of nonclinical toxicology studies pro-
vides a better integration of the safety profile.
The Safety Pharmacology Society has published its view 
of the distinctions between nonclinical toxicology and Safety 
Pharmacology approaches [293] and provides a perspective 
of where Safety Pharmacology fits in the Drug Development 
cycle. Pugsley et al. [294] provide a further differentiation in 
that Safety Pharmacology includes an expectation to identify 
the probability of rare lethal events, providing a somewhat 
unique characteristic of Safety Pharmacology.
Pharmacologic proﬁling was initially focused on guiding the 
synthetic chemist in the discovery of new pharmacologically 
active chemicals, rather than on the detection of adverse drug 
effects in humans [32]. Historically, in reviewing the common 
adverse drug ﬁndings in humans, Zbinden [32] pointed out that 
some responses are easily detected in both humans and experi-
mental animals (e.g., sedation, anorexia, body weight changes, 
tremor, tachycardia), and some responses are only detectable 
in humans (e.g., tinnitus, vertigo, nausea, headache). In any 
event, when one considers the nature of the functional distur-
bances encountered in both nonclinical and clinical testing, 
it becomes evident that pharmacologic proﬁling is critical to 
the safety evaluation of potential therapeutic agents [295,296]. 
Every chemical that enters the body has the potential for cre-
ating effects that may or may not be related to its pharmaco-
logic activity. Antihistamines, for example, produce sedation, 
which is related to their desired pharmacologic effects, and 
anticholinergic responses, which are not [295]. Pharmacologic 
proﬁling can help identify the potential incidence of effects 
unrelated to the known pharmacologic activity. Pharmacologic 
proﬁling also provides crucial information for the selection 
of NCEs during the early discovery process, for the design of 
toxicology studies, and for the approach to safety monitoring 
appropriate for clinical trials [67,297]. Traditionally, it is the 
objective of Safety Pharmacology to identify risks associated 
with the pharmacological characteristics of a particular agent 
(some would call this toxicology), essentially at therapeutically 
relevant doses and potentially over time, if indicated. There is 
an advantage in the active integration of basic pharmacology 
and Safety Pharmacology approaches; they need to be con-
ducted independently, but with active communication around 
the outcomes. In the basic pharmacology assessments, imaging 
technologies can be used to provide longitudinal assessments 
of compound effects in many therapeutic areas. In cardiovas-
cular pharmacology, the use of echocardiography (ultra-sound) 
provides an assessment of drug-induced changes in cardiac 
function and is used in both safety and efficacy studies to great 
advantage [298]. Advances in technology utilized in Safety 
Pharmacology evaluations include the use of external telem-
etry, with the advantage of surgery not being required [299].
Again, from a historical perspective, the Japanese Ministry 
of Health and Welfare had published guidelines for gen-
eral pharmacology studies (the original reference for Safety 
Pharmacology studies) [300]. These studies are designed to 
characterize effects and potency and to determine the mecha-
nism. The guidelines include studies to determine effects on 
general activity and behavior, the CNS, the autonomic nervous 
system and smooth muscle, the respiratory and cardiovascular 
systems, the gastrointestinal tract, and renal excretion.
The ICH guidelines now include select pharmacology guid-
ance (Table 8.6). ICH Topic S7A [289] addresses the deﬁnition, 
objectives, and scope of Safety Pharmacology studies, as well 
as studies required prior to Phase I clinical trials and prior 
to marketing approval. The Safety Pharmacology core battery 
has been built based on previously existing regional draft guid-
ance [188] and now includes the following vital organ systems:
•	 CNS: motor, behavior, reflex, body temperature
•	 Cardiovascular system: blood pressure, heart rate, 
electrocardiogram (ECG), hERG
•	 Respiratory system: rate, tidal volume, O2 saturation
Follow-up studies are recommended to provide a greater depth 
of understanding of the effects observed in the core battery 
or from clinical trials on pharmacovigilance: learning and 
memory, cardiac output, ventricular contractility vascular 
resistance, pulmonary compliance and resistance, pulmonary 
arterial pressure, renal/urinary system, autonomic nervous 
system, gastrointestinal system, dependency potential, skel-
etal muscle, and immune and endocrine functions; in vitro

371
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
and in vivo approaches can be used in concert [289,301]. A 
survey of industry trends in CNS Safety Pharmacology was 
published by the Safety Pharmacology Society [302]. They 
reported that the rat was the most common species used in the 
assessment of seizure, conduction, and abuse liability. Also, 
relative to nonclinical CNS adverse signs, the most frequent 
findings were seizures, gait abnormalities, tremors, emesis, 
sedation, and salivation [302]. Moser [303] provided an analy-
sis and description of the assays available for neurotoxicity 
which address both the core ICH battery and follow-up stud-
ies. Neurotoxicology has evolved from the time when histo-
pathology was considered the gold standard to a realization 
that changes in system function, as well as structure, can help 
us understand adverse events in the CNS [303]. Functional 
assessments have the advantage of assessing the same param-
eters longitudinally.
The EU Committee for Propriety Medicine Products 
(CPMP) had published a Points to Consider document on 
QT interval prolongation [304]. Please note, the CPMP was 
replaced by The Committee for Medicinal Products for 
Human Use (CHMP) in 2004 [305]. The impact of QT prolon-
gation and related occurrence of Torsades de Pointes, with the 
use of quinolones, has been reviewed by Frothingham [306]. 
The ICH Topic S7B [301] now provides recommendations for 
nonclinical studies to address the potential for QT interval pro-
longation. It also provides guidance on integrated risk assess-
ments. The prolongation of the QT interval is one of the few 
single physiological responses that could end the development 
of a potential new pharmaceutical [307].
8.7.5  Clinical Trials in Pediatric Populations
The FDA has found that most products indicated for the treat-
ment of diseases that occur in both adults and children have 
little specific nonclinical support for pediatric use. As a result, 
a regulation requiring pediatric studies for certain NCEs 
and NBEs had been proposed [308] and updated [309]. The 
CHMP [305] had also concluded that speciﬁc age-dependent 
differences in PK, pharmacodynamics, growth process and 
development, and speciﬁc pathology require that therapeutic 
agents be tested in the target age group [310]. The EMA has 
published a guidance covering the conduct of juvenile animal 
studies in support of pediatric indications [311]. The ICH has 
recommended that pediatric clinical trials be supported by 
repeated-dose toxicity studies of an appropriate duration, all 
developmental toxicity studies, and the full battery of geno-
toxicity tests. These studies should be concluded before the 
pediatric clinical trials begin [123]. Due to the potentially 
long duration of treatment, carcinogenicity studies must be 
considered prior to the initiation of long-term pediatric clini-
cal trials. The performance of nonclinical studies in juvenile 
animals may also be necessary if previous toxicology evalua-
tions and human safety data are not sufﬁcient or raise a cause 
for concern. In the relatively new guidance on the nonclinical 
evaluation of anticancer drugs [185], it has been stated that the 
general approach to investigating anticancer drugs in pediatric 
patients is first to define a relatively safe dose in adult popula-
tions. This is the general approach that has existed for including 
pediatric populations for many years. For oncology indications 
specifically, juvenile animal toxicology studies are not usually 
conducted to support the inclusion of pediatric populations. 
Morford et al. [312] have reviewed the strategies, challenges, 
and current practice of providing nonclinical safety evalua-
tions for the inclusion of pediatric populations in the devel-
opment of biopharmaceuticals. Pediatric safety assessments 
supported by nonclinical safety evaluations are now required 
for every NDA and Biologics Licensing Application in the 
United States and for Marketing Authorization Applications 
in Europe [312].
More recently, the ICH guidance E11(R1) addressed the 
issues surrounding clinical trials in pediatric populations 
[313], addressing the ethics of pediatric clinical investiga-
tions advances in pediatric drug development. It is realized 
that pediatric and adult populations have significant differ-
ences related to continually developing organ systems and 
that existing data from adult animals and humans might be 
insufficient to address safety in pediatric trials [314]. A new 
guidance from ICH, S11, has reached Step 4 and is intended 
to harmonize nonclinical safety assessment and support 
development for pediatric indications [315]. ICH S11 takes a 
weight-of-evidence approach in determining the necessity of 
additional nonclinical studies to support pediatric indications. 
The weight of evidence is based on [315]:
•	 Youngest intended patient age
•	 Effects on developing organ systems
•	 Amount/type of existing data (e.g., data supporting 
adult clinical trials)
•	 Pharmacological target has a role in organ 
development
•	 Selectivity and specificity of pharmaceutical
•	 Clinical treatment duration
One of the significant outcomes is the inclusion of a juvenile 
animal study in the safety profile. Relative to the weight of 
evidence, the guidance includes tables of age-dependent devel-
opment for humans, rat, dog, minipig, NHP, with a specific 
comparison of rat and human organ development categorized 
as neonate, infant, child and adolescent. The design of and 
points to consider in juvenile animal studies have been covered 
by Cappon et al. [316] and Kim et al. [317]. As in all other drug 
development programs consultation with the relevant regula-
tory agency, for example, FDA is recommended.
8.7.6  Nonclinical Evaluation of Anticancer Drugs
As discussed earlier, the development of drugs for life-threat-
ening diseases such as cancer and AIDS requires a modiﬁcation 
of the approach established for the standard safety assessment 
of NCEs or NBEs. The treatment of cancer usually includes 
the use of potent agents designed to halt cell replication. The 
therapeutic index for these agents is often small. Due to the 
life-threatening nature of the disease, a greater tolerance of 
drug toxicity and a shorter nonclinical testing strategy are 
generally accepted for therapeutic agents in this class, as seri-
ous drug effects are often less threatening than the targeted 
disease; however, due to the greater intrinsic toxicity of the

372
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
agents in this class, the early clinical trials are often conducted 
in patients rather than normal volunteers as with other thera-
peutic agents.
The history of anticancer drug development has been exten-
sively reviewed [318–320]. The basic approach for develop-
ment of anticancer drugs includes:
•	 Establishment of a safe clinical trial entry dose
•	 Determination of potential dose-limiting target-
organ toxicity
•	 Evaluation of reversibility of effects
•	 Determination of MTD in animals
•	 Determination of dose schedule toxicity
Nonclinical studies have been useful in predicting MTDs in 
humans, and safe starting doses for clinical trials [70,319,321]. 
The CHMP [305] had provided a speciﬁc note for guidance on 
the nonclinical evaluation of anticancer agents [322]. Safety 
Pharmacology is generally required prior to Phase I studies, as 
are the determinations of Cmax (maximum plasma concentra-
tion of the drug) and AUC at the animal MTD. Other kinetic 
parameters are expected to be determined prior to Phase II/III 
testing. Determination of the single-dose MTD in rodents and 
the approximate MTD in nonrodents, using a relevant route of 
exposure, is also expected prior to Phase I. Repeated-dose tox-
icity studies in two rodent species are expected prior to Phase 
I clinical studies. Longer term repeat-dose studies, in a rodent 
and nonrodent species, that are equal in duration to the clini-
cal trial but less than 6 months, are expected prior to Phase 
II/III. Genotoxicity testing is not necessary prior to Phase I/
II, but the genotoxicity battery is expected to be conducted 
prior to Phase II/III. Because cytotoxic anticancer agents are 
known to have an adverse effect on reproduction, developmen-
tal toxicity studies are generally not required but are encour-
aged. The development of anticancer drugs and other therapies 
for life-threatening diseases has unique characteristics based 
on the nature of the disease and the inherent toxicity of the 
therapeutic agents.
ICH guidance S9 [185] addresses the international consensus 
on the nonclinical evaluation of pharmaceuticals for the treat-
ment, but not for the prevention, of advanced cancer. The guid-
ance [185] applies to both NCEs and NBEs. As stated above in 
the historical review, the development of therapy for advanced 
cancer requires a good deal of flexibility in the application of 
established nonclinical approaches. Related to clinical trial 
duration and treatment schedules, ICH S9 provides a general 
roadmap [185]. The nonclinical evaluation calls for the devel-
opment of a nonclinical proof of principle as established by in 
vivo pharmacology studies of efficacy and in pharmacologic 
evaluations of effects on the heart, lungs, and CNSs. In the 
general toxicology approaches, determination of the NOEL or 
NOAEL is not essential. The toxicology studies are designed 
to support the schedule of administration in the clinical trials 
and to help with an assessment of reversibility of (or recovery 
from) adverse events. Reproductive toxicology and genotoxic-
ity evaluations are generally required for support of market-
ing. The study of carcinogenicity is not specifically required. 
It is recommended that the readers review ICH S9 [185] for 
information on kinetics, combined therapies, pediatric popula-
tions, duration of toxicology studies to support clinical trials, 
and the associated dosing schedules, etc.
8.7.7  Alternative Methods for 
Carcinogenicity Determination
The testing for carcinogenic potential has relied primarily on 
the 2-year rodent bioassay. Recently, through the ICH process, 
the rat has been identiﬁed as either the most acceptable or 
most relevant model for the 2-year bioassay. In addition to the 
rat bioassay, an alternative short-term model of carcinogenic-
ity evaluation is recommended [152]. These approaches may 
include studies in transgenic mice (the rasH2.Tg model is a 
likely alternative).
To evaluate and verify the available alternative models, the 
ILSI had initiated a collaborative effort among industry, aca-
demia, and government laboratories to study chosen chemicals 
in the alternative models and evaluate the results in light of 
the known bioassay data. It is highly recommended that any 
deviation from the standard two species (mouse and rat) carci-
nogenicity bioassay approach be discussed with the FDA prior 
to implementation.
The validated transgenic animal models may be used as 
follows:
•	 To conﬁrm results in equivocal 2-year rodent 
bioassays
•	 To set priorities for 2-year carcinogenicity bioassays
•	 As an alternative to the mouse 2-year bioassay, in 
conjunction with the rat 2-year bioassay
•	 To assess the carcinogenic potential of new geno-
toxic contaminants or degradants in a drug product 
after 2-year bioassays are completed
In addition, the use of transgenic animals may support weight-
of-evidence decisions; they are relatively short term and are 
generally less expensive than the 2-year rodent bioassay.
Several transgenic mouse models are available to comple-
ment the rat 2-year bioassay. The Tg.AC transgenic mouse, 
the heterozygous p53+/– gene-deﬁcient mouse, and the Tg/
rasH2 mouse are discussed as examples. The Tg.AC mouse 
model [323] examines chemically initiated skin as a target 
for tumorigenesis. The Tg.AC line may be able to differ-
entiate carcinogens from noncarcinogens but may not be 
able to differentiate genotoxic carcinogens from those that 
cause only tumor promotion activity. This model may only 
be useful in combination with other transgenic animal mod-
els [324]. The heterozygous p53+/– deﬁcient mouse model 
is based on rendering mice heterozygous for the p53 tumor 
suppressor gene [177]. These animals are at elevated risk for 
tumor development. The model has been proposed to best 
approximate humans at risk for heritable forms of cancer. 
This model may be able to detect genotoxic carcinogens in 
a 6-month period. The Tg/rasH2 transgenic mouse contains 
multiple copies of the human c-Ha-ras gene. It has been 
found to respond well to both genotoxic and nongenotoxic 
carcinogens [325].

373
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
The neonatal mouse assay has been available longer than 
the transgenic animal models. The detailed protocol for this 
1-year study has been previously reviewed [326]. Neonates are 
treated with the test compound on days 8 and 15 of age and 
then observed until 1 year of age. At that time, the animals 
are evaluated for tumor production. This assay is sensitive to 
direct-acting carcinogens, primarily those that work through 
formation of covalently bound DNA adducts.
The rodent bioassay has been used for over 30 years and 
has provided useful data, although it is not a perfect system 
and has received much criticism. The investigation of alterna-
tives to the standard bioassay in two rodent species has been 
encouraged by the ICH. Conducting a 2-year study in the rat 
and an alternative study in the mouse provides an acceptable 
transition. The available alternative models generally accepted 
as useful in carcinogenicity testing include the Tg/rasH2 trans-
genic mouse and the p53+/– knockout mouse. The Tg/rasH2 
model is preferred for testing nongenotoxic compounds, and 
the p53+/– knockout model is preferred for testing genotoxic 
compounds. The general utility of available alternative models 
of carcinogenicity testing has been reviewed by MacDonald 
et al. [325]. Alternative assay results should be included in a 
weight-of-evidence approach for risk assessment [325]. A his-
torical perspective on the industry’s experience with alterna-
tive carcinogenicity models has been provided by Ashton et al. 
[172]. An additional alternative approach to carcinogenic risk 
evaluation, focusing on chemical exposure and involving a 
combination of in vitro assays, computer models, and short-
term (≤13 weeks) studies rather than the 2-year rodent bioas-
say, had been proposed by Cohen [327].
8.7.8  Cell and Gene Therapy Product  
Development
Cell and Gene Therapy (CGT) are overlapping fields of 
research. Gene Therapy specifically involvers the transfer of 
genetic material via a vector, to treat or cure inherited func-
tional deficits, whereas cell therapy involves the transfer of 
cells to replace damaged or non-functional cells; the goals are 
similar. Gene Therapy is promising for cancer, cystic fibrosis, 
heart disease, diabetes inherited disorders, infectious disease; 
cell therapy finds application in cancer, autoimmunity, infec-
tious disease, rebuilding areas of cellular damage. The history, 
mechanism of action, indication, clinical trials, and safety of 
twenty selected CGTs have been reviewed [329].
As a concept CGT has had a long road, which has some-
times been troubled, one of the gaps in this area is the coor-
dination of the sub-disciplines in terms of understanding the 
pharmacology and toxicity of this diverse class of therapies 
[329]. Adverse and even fatal iatrogenic complications have 
muted the initial enthusiasm around anticipated clinical suc-
cess [330]. The unique concerns highlighted by this early 
experience were related to unregulated transgene expres-
sion, long-term persistence, and off-target biodistribution. 
Bolt et al. [331] reported on a pharmaceutical/biotechnology 
industry survey conducted by the European Federation of 
Pharmaceutical Industries and Associates. They reviewed the 
design of nonclinical studies, for example, toxicity end points, 
biodistribution, insertion assessments, immunogenicity, Safety 
Pharmacology, and regulatory interactions. The review listed 
challenges to development, for example, are long-term stud-
ies always necessary, dealing with immunogenicity, regulatory 
consensus, and guidance for late-stage development [331].
Publications on Gene Therapy began in the early 1970s, and 
the first Gene Therapy product, utilized in cancer therapy was 
approved in China in 2003. Since then, the rate of develop-
ment has grown rapidly. EMA approved its first genetic ther-
apy product, for pancreatitis in 2012, the FDA approved its 
first genetic therapy product, for melanoma, in 2015. One of 
the initial lessons learned is that despite the lifesaving efficacy 
of the therapy, the cost of the therapy impacts its success; for 
those interested in this twist, addressing the impact of cost to 
the patient and success is reviewed by Curan [332]. One of the 
greatest concerns for CGT is safety and the development of 
safer and more effective vectors [333,334].
There is a large body of regulatory literature covering the 
area of CGT products. This area was extensively reviewed 
[335], and the reader is encouraged to read through the cov-
erage of international regulatory requirements, nonclinical 
approaches, meetings, and challenges in safety and quality. 
The specific advice given related to the U.S. FDA is that the 
approach is flexible and based on the incorporation of basic 
toxicology principles familiar in nonclinical testing environ-
ments. There is no standard set of studies applicable to all 
CGT products, the flexibility translates to case-by-case deci-
sions. Specifically, “toxicology studies should be sufficiently 
comprehensive to permit identification, characterization, and 
quantitation of potential local and systemic toxicities, their 
onset, dose response and reversibility” [335]. The relevance of 
the animal safety model is a concern.
As mentioned above, the international guidance and expec-
tation for assessing the safety of CGT products are extensive. 
The guidance positions of the U.S. FDA, the EMA, and the 
planning of ICH are examples of the expectations of regu-
latory bodies. In 1998, the FDA published guidance for the 
industry on CGT [336], establishing the basic principles and 
expectations for nonclinical evaluation of CGT products. The 
principles expressed were:
•	 Safe starting dose and escalation plans for clinical 
trials
•	 Identification of target organs
•	 Parameters in clinical trials
•	 Determine at-risk populations
The nonclinical studies should take into consideration:
•	 Population of cells to be administered
•	 Vector used
•	 Animal model relevance
•	 Relevance of disease models
•	 Intended dose, route, and regimen
The guidance also pointed out that conventional pharmacol-
ogy and toxicology testing may not always be appropriate for 
CGT. In addition, the guidance recognized that while GLP

374
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
principles should be followed as closely as possible, nonclini-
cal studies in support of CGT product clinical trials may not 
always strictly adhere to GLP regulations. Nonclinical test-
ing should employ an appropriate animal model, for exam-
ple, pharmacologically responsive, including animal models 
of disease if appropriate. Nonclinical studies addressing the 
pharmacodynamic properties of CGT (in vitro plus in vivo) are 
important to understanding therapeutic activity and inform-
ing safety assessments [337]. In 2013, the FDA issued a guid-
ance for preclinical assessment of CGT [339], this guidance 
expanded on the principles stated in 1998 and provided addi-
tional information on the expectations for selection of animal 
models, including animal models of disease; recommendation 
for a study of the detailed relevance of the species selected and 
the disease model in support of clinical trials. The 2013 guid-
ance expanded on the primary considerations for toxicology 
study design, including:
•	 Clinical indication
•	 Quality of preclinical or clinical safety information
•	 Quality of existing pharmacology or proof of con-
cept (POC) data
•	 Previous preclinical/clinical experience
•	 mechanism of action (MOA) of the CGT product
•	 Intrinsic properties of the CGT product
•	 Pathophysiology of animal disease/injury model if 
used
The 2013 guidance provides the same advice on GLP com-
pliance for nonclinical studies supporting clinical trials. In 
2019, EMA published a Draft Guideline for nonclinical and 
clinical requirements for investigational advanced therapy 
products [339]. The EMA guideline has similar intent to the 
FDA Guidance, relative to what is important in toxicology 
studies and the relevance of the animal model used, and 
the recognition that with molecules of this class, it may not 
always be possible to fully conform to GLPs. EMA empha-
sizes that the toxicology studies should be designed to pro-
duce meaningful data in support of clinical trials. EMA 
acknowledges the use of single-dose toxicology studies, as 
appropriate, but that the duration of toxicology studies in 
support of a cell-based product intended to replace a tis-
sue and become an integral part of the body, should have 
a case-by-case determination. In the development of cell-
based products in Europe, the development of an immune 
response in nonclinical studies, with the clinical candidate, 
can lead to a decision that nonclinical testing may not be 
necessary [340]. The ICH does not have a specific guidance 
for nonclinical biodistribution of Gene Therapy products. 
The ICH Expert Working Group has published a concept 
paper addressing the intent to resolve the lack of harmoniza-
tion in biodistribution studies for Gene Therapy products, 
this guidance is expected in 2023 [341].
Ultimately, the purpose of the nonclinical safety evaluations 
is to support the conduct of a clinical trial, and these occur in 
phases, as indicated previously. Using the FDA as an example, 
the initiation of a clinical trial requires a number of discus-
sions with a regulatory agency, and in this case with FDA. The 
preparation of an IND application for CGT products was pre-
sented in 2018 [342,343]. The interactions with FDA per phase 
of development are summarized as follows:
•	 Nonclinical discovery phase: INTERACT, INitial 
Targeted Engagement for Regulatory Advice on 
CBER producTs, informal meeting
•	 Nonclinical phase: pre-IND meeting, don’t ignore 
pre-IND input from FDA
•	 Clinical phase: End of Phase I and end of Phase 
II meetings, Pre-BLA meeting (Biologics License 
Application) at end Phase III
•	 Marketing application phase: BLA, permis-
sion to introduce biologic into interstate com-
merce; PEDUFA V, safety plan post marketing 
surveillance
•	 Post marketing phase: Safety meetings
Traditional toxicology programs are generally challenged 
in designing nonclinical toxicity approaches supporting the 
safety of CGT products. While the general approach of non-
clinical safety of CGT products parallels that of conventional 
biologic agents, there are differences that must be considered. 
Both require the use of the most pharmacologically relevant 
model or a model that mimics the clinical disease. CGT prod-
ucts may not have such models. The basic principles of evalu-
ating dose response with an effect level and an NOAEL by a 
relevant route of administration are part of CGT product devel-
opment. The toxicology studies should include a dose equiv-
alent to the clinical dose and a dose exceeding those in the 
clinical trials [344,345]. The nonclinical safety profile should 
consider the risk of insertional oncogenesis, gene expression, 
and vector-related nontherapeutic proteins [346]. When unex-
pected biodistribution is observed with CGT products, studies 
of gene expression should be conducted and an assessment of 
whether the presence of the gene is associated with adverse 
effects should be made.
For Gene Therapy products, specifically, the safety of the 
viral vector should be established, as well as the toxicity of 
expressed proteins [345]. There is no single approach to non-
clinical studies of viral vectors [347], recommendations have 
been made for approaches to chronic toxicity and the appli-
cation of standard mutagenicity and genotoxicity (traditional 
approach not recommended). EFD studies may eventually 
be required to investigate the ability of the adeno-associated 
virus to pass through the placental barrier [346].
Nonclinical animal models for CGT development have con-
sistently been an area for discussion and debate. Regulatory 
guidance is in general agreement that the animal species 
selected for a nonclinical program should demonstrate a phar-
macological response to the CGT product under evaluation, 
and that the nonclinical safety program should generally be 
similar to that used for traditional pharmaceuticals. The U.S. 
FDA lists a number of factors to be considered in selecting the 
relevant species [338]:

375
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
•	 Physiology and anatomy similar to humans
•	 Susceptibility to infection and replication of viral or 
microbial vectors
•	 Immune tolerance to human cell therapy product 
or human transgene expressed by Gene Therapy 
product
•	 Ability to use clinical delivery system and delivery 
regimen
Animal homologs [348] and non-traditional species, such as 
transgenic or knockout rodents, and large animals, such as 
sheep, pigs, goats, and horses, may be used to evaluate CGT 
safety and efficacy, with justification. For CGT products, there 
is no default decision for the use of NHPs, the use of rodent 
and nonrodent species, or the use of multiple species [349].
In a review of animal models for CGT studies, Gopinath 
et al. [350] reviewed both large and small animal models rela-
tive to the following: handling and maintenance, rodents are 
easier and less expensive; phenotype expression, small ani-
mals partially demonstrate human parameters, larger animals 
more closely mirror human disease; disease modeling, small 
animals generate disease models more quickly; longitudinal 
studies, small animals have shorter life span; therapeutic inter-
pretation, small animal dose adjustment difficult to transfer to 
clinic. In terms of utilizing animal models for development of 
CGT for various organ systems, a recommendation of utilizing 
both large and small animal models, for example, rodent to 
canine to humans, is presented [350]. The use of animal mod-
els of disease in nonclinical safety studies has several potential 
limitations that must be recognized. The limitations are related 
to availability of historical controls, heterogeneity of disease 
expression, limited life span, and confounding effect of the 
disease on the assessment of drug effects [351]. Researchers 
in the area of CGT product safety recommend continu-
ing to use the standard animal models in a tiered approach 
to fully understand safety, with the traditional nonclinical 
safety approach being Tier 1; animal models of disease being 
reserved to address specific mode of action questions [351]. 
The literature is generally in agreement that larger animal spe-
cies, for example, rabbits, canines, NHPs and farm animals are 
better predictors of human response than rodents [352,353]. 
Overall, there are many relevant animal models from fish and 
traditional animal models to genetically modified models and 
goats, pigs, cows, and horses, for the evaluation of CGT prod-
ucts [354].
As with all areas of pharmaceutical research and develop-
ment, it is excellent advice to engage with a relevant regulatory 
body to discuss the justification for the many decisions to be 
made when evaluating the safety of CGT. It is also important 
to remember that even if the animal models chosen/available 
do not create an exact duplicate of the human response, useful 
information can still be gathered [355].
8.7.9  Nonclinical Vaccine Development
Vaccines have been recognized as an effective public health 
tool for many years. The development, and use, of vaccines 
has resulted in a worldwide reduction of disease and mortality 
through community protection resulting in an increase in the 
global quality of life. As with all pharmaceuticals and biologi-
cals, vaccines are not 100% safe. The FDA and global regula-
tory bodies require that vaccines be both safe and effective and 
without significant adverse effects. This requires a rigorous 
and extensive development program that includes nonclini-
cal and clinical studies to evaluate safety and efficacy. Even 
though marketed vaccines are safe and effective and save mil-
lions of lives each year, some individuals have shown adverse 
effects following vaccine administration. As a result, there are 
individuals who do not accept or refuse vaccines due to fear of 
potential side effects or because of philosophical or religious 
objection (these are more difficult to address with scientific 
evidence). The refusal to vaccinate, by population subsets, 
has been placed general populations at increased risk, and has 
resulted in outbreaks of polio, hepatitis B, and measles in mul-
tiple countries, including the United States [356]. Therefore, it 
is the obligation of vaccine developers to provide transparency 
and evidence of safety and efficacy to the general population 
in order to avoid or at least minimize objections to vaccina-
tion. The American College of Toxicology presented a con-
tinuing education session on the safety evaluation of vaccines 
at its 39th annual meeting, covering European regulations, 
risk assessment, dose site–related findings, and a series of case 
studies [357]. This section will present a high-level overview 
of expectations for the conduct of vaccine safety studies.
There are several definitions to be aware of when discussing 
vaccines:
•	 A vaccine is made from inactivated or attenuated 
organisms or from fragments, subunits, or puri-
fied subcomponents of these organisms that pro-
vide antigens that stimulate the immune system to 
produce antibodies (humoral immunity) or to acti-
vate immune cells (cellular immunity) but does not 
induce the disease.
•	 An antigen is a foreign substance that induces 
humoral or cellular immunity through production of 
antibodies by B lymphocytes and activation of spe-
cific T lymphocytes.
•	 An adjuvant is an agent that enhances the effect of an 
antigen by increasing the immune response or that 
can modify innate cellular immunity. They may be 
added to a vaccine to boost the immune response 
which can minimize the dose of the antigen required 
to produce an immune response.
8.7.9.1  A Brief Vaccine History
A brief history of observations and developing science around 
vaccines seems to be a good starting point. Acquired immu-
nity from the plague was first described by Thucydides in 430 
BC in his History of the Peloponnesian War [358]. The rec-
ognition that an affected individual might pass on a disease 
to another individual who is not yet affected has a long and

376
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
varied history. Variolation (for the prevention of smallpox) was 
developed in Asia (~10th century) but did not become estab-
lished in Europe until the 1700s. Immunology, itself, as a sci-
ence did not become established until the late 1800s.
Edward Jenner is credited with introducing vaccination with 
cowpox virus to induce immunity to smallpox which became 
the first vaccine in the mid-1700s. By the turn of the last cen-
tury, cholera, typhoid, plague, and anthrax vaccines were 
developed. Between 1890 and 1950, many vaccines for diph-
theria and pertussis, as well as a toxoid vaccine for tetanus, 
were developed. Tissue culture methods between 1950 and 
1985 led to the Salk and Sabin vaccines, along with measles, 
mumps, and rubella. Application of molecular genetics has 
resulted in the proliferation of vaccines for hepatitis B vac-
cines, Hemophilus influenzae b, the less reactogenic acellular 
pertussis vaccine, new techniques for seasonal influenza vac-
cine manufacture, and advances in the development of many 
DNA and RNA vaccines, new adjuvants, TB vaccines, human 
papilloma virus, CMV vaccines, RSV vaccines, staphylococ-
cal and streptococcal disease, rotavirus, herpes zoster, pan-
demic flu, shigella, HIV, and most recently Ebola, Zika and 
COVID-19. Development of vaccines for allergies, autoim-
mune disorders, Alzheimer’s disease, and for the treatment of 
addictions is in progress [359]. The development of vaccines 
has generally taken a long path, however, as will be briefly 
discussed below, the 2019 recognition of the COVID-19 infec-
tion has accelerated the development of vaccines based on the 
recognition of the risk represented by a global pandemic.
8.7.9.2  Types of Vaccines
Vaccines are prepared from inactivated (killed) or live attenu-
ated (weakened) bacteria, viruses or parasites, fragments or 
subunits of these agents, conjugated subcomponents, DNA 
plasmid, and RNA. Weakening viruses result in limited repro-
duction once inside cells so that no infection (or in some cases 
a mild infection) occurs but antibody production is stimulated 
along with immune memory. The advantage is that one or two 
doses usually provide life-long immunity, but these vaccines 
can’t be given to people with weakened immune systems. 
Inactivating a virus prevents replication and prevents even mild 
infections but can still stimulate the immune system and can 
be given to people with weakened immune systems. However, 
several doses are typically required to achieve immunity. 
Using subcomponents or fractions of a virus (typically a pro-
tein on the viral capsid) generally results in long-lived immu-
nity after two doses and can be given to people with weakened 
immune systems; viral capsids lacking genetic material are 
also used. DNA plasmid vaccines contain one or more DNA 
sequences capable of inducing an immune response but a care-
ful evaluation of the potential for biodistribution, persistence, 
and integration into the host genome must be conducted. Viral-
vectored vaccines are live viruses such as adenoviruses that 
express a heterologous antigen derived from an organism that 
has no effective vaccine. Toxins produced by a bacterium can 
be inactivated to produce a toxoid, and the polysaccharide coat 
of some bacteria can also be conjugated to a harmless protein 
to produce a vaccine conjugate.
8.7.9.3  Vaccine Development and Approval
The history, definitions and vaccine approaches are more fully 
addressed in other publications, the purpose here is to address 
the nonclinical development of vaccines and to provide general 
information of approaches, regulatory positions, and risks in 
vaccine development. Vaccine development follows the same 
general pathway for approval as that of pharmaceuticals. The 
details of the nonclinical development of vaccines and vac-
cines containing adjuvants have been published by the WHO 
[360,361]. These guidelines are widely used as the standard for 
designing vaccine safety studies although specific countries 
such as China, Japan, and India have other guidelines outlin-
ing their specific requirements. There are also guidance docu-
ments for specific requirements for administration to pregnant 
women or WOCBP [362] as well as DNA vaccines, recombi-
nant DNA vaccines, combination vaccines, and viral-vectored 
vaccines [363]. Initial studies of vaccine safety and evidence 
of efficacy occur in nonclinical studies, principally safety and 
immunogenicity, which precede clinical trials. The approval 
process for a new vaccine can take many years. However, new 
FDA guidance [364] has resulted in a more rapid approval of 
vaccines in response to public health emergencies to permit 
rapid initiation of clinical trials and emergency use authori-
zation following submission of findings to the Vaccines and 
Related Biological Products Advisory Committee [365].
The Ebola vaccine took over 10 years to develop. Until 
2020, the shortest approval process for a vaccine had been 
for mumps, which took 4 years. Development of vaccines for 
infectious diseases with broad global impact can proceed in 
12–18 months, or less. In response to the COVID-19 pandemic, 
the time required to develop the first vaccines to prevent 
COVID-19 infection and to obtain emergency use authoriza-
tion from the FDA was less than a year following the release of 
the SARS-CoV-2 genetic sequence by the Chinese government 
on 10 January 2020.
Because of cost and high attrition rates, vaccine develop-
ers have typically followed a linear sequence of steps, with 
multiple pauses for data analysis or manufacturing process 
checks, to mitigate the substantial development risk. The rapid 
development of vaccines in response to the COVID-19 pan-
demic, however, involves the conduct of multiple steps in par-
allel [366]. For example, clinical trials may be able to proceed 
in parallel with nonclinical studies when the vaccine platform 
has been used previously and is familiar to the FDA. As men-
tioned previously, the rapid development of a vaccine with 
nonclinical and clinical studies performed in parallel requires 
transparency on the part of the developer in order to build con-
fidence in both safety and efficacy.
As part of the approval process, the FDA requires that the 
manufacturer provide a list of all ingredients. New ingredi-
ents or ingredients with less experience must be fully evalu-
ated for safety. Many vaccines contain adjuvants that enhance 
the immune response following vaccination. For example, 
aluminum salts such as aluminum hydroxide, aluminum phos-
phate, alum (potassium aluminum sulfate), or mixed alumi-
num salts have been used for at least 100 years and are the 
most widely used. However, they can produce granulomas and 
are not considered ideal adjuvants due to lack of consistent

377
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
absorption [367]. More powerful adjuvants have been devel-
oped to compensate for reduced immune responses observed 
with fragments or subunits of viruses and permit a reduction 
in the amount of administered antigen. These newer adjuvants 
include AS04 which is a combination of aluminum hydrox-
ide and monophosphoryl lipid A (MPL). The AS03 adjuvant is 
made up of the compounds, D,L-alpha-tocopherol (vitamin E) 
and squalene. MF59 is also an oil-in-water emulsion of squa-
lene oil. CpG 1018 and CpG 7909 are based on synthetic DNA 
sequences. AS01B is composed of MPL, a purified fat-like 
substance, and QS-21, which is purified from the bark of an 
evergreen tree. Other adjuvants in vaccines under development 
include poly I:C, flagellin, and Icosamatrix [363]. Even though 
many adjuvants have been tested, only a few are currently used 
in approved vaccines.
Other vaccine components include antibiotics to help prevent 
bacterial contamination during manufacturing. Formaldehyde 
is used to inactivate viruses so that they don’t cause disease 
(e.g., polio virus used to make polio vaccine) and to detoxify 
bacterial toxins, such as the toxin used to make diphtheria 
vaccine. Sugars, amino acids, and proteins are added as sta-
bilizers that help protect the vaccine from adverse conditions. 
Preservatives may be added to some vaccine formulations to 
prevent the growth of bacteria or fungi that may be introduced 
into the vaccine during its use, for example, repeated puncture 
of a multi-dose vaccine vial with a needle. In the manufacture 
of viral vaccines, the virus may be grown in cells which uti-
lize fetal bovine serum. The local reactogenicity of vaccines is 
likely due to these excipients and/or the adjuvant.
Typically, the vaccine approval process includes the linear 
steps of
•	 An IND application
•	 Pre-licensure vaccine clinical trials
•	 A BLA
•	 Inspection of manufacturing facility
•	 Presentation of findings to FDA’s Vaccines and 
Related Biological Products Advisory Committee
•	 Usability testing of product labeling in adequately 
communicating both benefit and risk
Pre-licensure vaccine clinical trials, following nonclinical 
safety evaluations, are typically conducted in three phases, as 
with any drug or biologic:
•	 Phase 1, initial human studies
•	 Safety and immunogenicity studies performed 
in a small number of closely monitored subjects
•	 Phase 2, dose-ranging studies
•	 May enroll hundreds of subjects
•	 Phase 3, enroll large numbers of individuals
•	 Provides critical documentation of efficacy and 
important additional safety data required for 
licensure
As with typical drug development procedures, if at any stage 
of the clinical or nonclinical studies there are significant con-
cerns about either safety or efficacy, the FDA may require 
additional nonclinical studies or may halt the clinical studies. 
If the phases of clinical development are successful, A BLA 
may be submitted. The BLA must provide the efficacy and 
safety information necessary for the FDA reviewers to assess 
risk/benefit. A review of the manufacturing facility is also 
conducted at this time.
8.7.9.4  Vaccine Toxicity
As mentioned previously, no pharmaceutical agent (including 
a vaccine) is 100% safe or is without side effects in certain 
individuals. Serious adverse events resulting in systemic toxic-
ity are rare. More commonly, vaccines produce local reacto-
genicity at the site of injection consisting of erythema and/or 
edema and pain. These are commonly seen with aluminum 
adjuvants. Sometimes, symptoms occur including elevation 
in body temperature with decreased appetite and body weight 
loss and represent effects of immune system stimulation lead-
ing to the release of inflammatory cytokines and an increase 
in fibrinogen. Other common observations in safety studies 
that are expected responses to immune stimulation include 
hyperplasia and hypertrophy of lymph nodes, increased abso-
lute and relative spleen weight and other organs of the reticu-
loendothelial system, changes in absolute and relative white 
blood cell count and differential, increased serum globulin, 
and decreased serum albumin. Some vaccines containing alu-
minum hydroxide have caused myofasiitis and myalgia [367] 
and some have resulted in the formation of granulomas. Other 
toxicities reported have included altered arterial flow, nega-
tive mood, enhanced disease, and autoimmune disease [367]. 
However, vaccines rarely result in serious local or systemic 
adverse effects, but there are toxicities related to the pres-
ence of an adjuvant or excipients in the vaccine formulation as 
described below.
8.7.9.5  Vaccine Nonclinical Safety Studies
Although approval of a vaccine at the beginning of vaccine 
development only required limited nonclinical evaluation, 
the FDA began to require more extensive evaluation nearly 
20 years ago. Single-, and more commonly, multiple-dose 
studies are now required by the FDA for both the antigen and 
adjuvant. Species selection is based on the desired clinical 
humoral or cellular immune response, but physiological and 
pharmacological translation to humans are also important 
considerations. Generally, only a single species is required for 
nonclinical safety studies. The design of these studies should 
also include the optimum number of animals/sex/group in 
both main and recovery groups for sufficient statistical power, 
the route of administration and volume of administration 
should mimic the proposed clinical route of administration, 
and the dose should be the maximum intended human clinical 
dose. The frequency of dose administration is usually similar 
to that of the clinical trial but can be compressed. The groups 
should include the vehicle/control article, the adjuvant/vehicle

378
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
alone, and the vaccine (antigen and adjuvant). Sometimes, a 
group containing the antigen/vehicle alone can be included. 
Typically, only a single-dose level is needed. Finally, the num-
ber of doses should be one more than that intended to be given 
in the clinic (N + 1) even if only a single dose will be given 
clinically, but the dosing intervals between successive doses 
may be compressed as mentioned previously.
Most vaccines are administered by intramuscular or SC 
injection. If multiple injections to a limb are given, they 
should be separated by at least 1 inch. However, other routes 
include dermal, intradermal, mucosal, intranasal, oral, buc-
cal, or sublingual that may utilize devices such as unit dose 
inhalers, nebulizers, transdermal patches, microneedles such 
as the MicronJet600 or Hollow Microstructured Transdermal 
System, and electroporation.
Each batch or lot of the vehicle/control article, adjuvant, or 
vaccine must be well characterized and have a Certificate of 
Analysis (CoA) or be characterized by the product label if it is 
a commercially available product. Stability under conditions 
of storage and use are required. Usually, the stability under 
conditions of storage from the time of manufacture through 
the last use is provided by a re-test date on the CoA. If not 
provided, the sponsor should provide a report to document 
storage stability. Stability of the adjuvant and vaccine under 
conditions of use (usually up to 4 hours on ice or at room tem-
perature) is also required. Typically, samples are collected at 
the time of first and last use. Samples are prepared and imme-
diately frozen or refrigerated and duplicates are held under 
conditions of use for the required time interval and then fro-
zen. Confirmation of the dose of administered antigen is also 
required if formulation of the antigen is required prior to dos-
ing. Stability and dose confirmation assays should be evalu-
ated using validated methods.
Standard protocol endpoints are included in these studies, 
but there are some differences in vaccine safety studies ver-
sus a small or large molecule safety study. Table 8.16 presents 
recommendations for design of a vaccine safety study and gen-
eral guidelines for development of a vaccine based on industry 
experience as well as WHO guidelines [360,361]. The com-
monly conducted nonclinical toxicology studies include [357]:
•	 Local tolerance
•	 Repeat-dose toxicology studies
•	 Immunogenicity
•	 Animal Rule Studies [368]
•	 Biodistribution
•	 Neurovirulence
•	 Attenuation
•	 Developmental and reproductive toxicology
•	 Adjuvants, depending on the experience and use, 
may also undergo genetic toxicology testing and pos-
sibly Safety Pharmacology
The most common animal species used in vaccine safety eval-
uations are the rat and the rabbit, although other species, for 
example, nonhuman primate are also used. The basic goals of 
the nonclinical studies are to establish safety, identify adver-
sity, identify parameters for clinical monitoring (biomarkers), 
and to support the safe continuation of clinical trials [357].
It should be noted that C-reactive protein is a useful bio-
marker of acute-phase response following administration of 
novel candidate vaccines [369].
The goal of a vaccine development program is to produce a 
safe and effective vaccine that produces a strong and durable 
immune response in the greatest number of people, with the 
fewest side effects, in a cost-effective manner for distribution 
in both developed and developing regions. Nonclinical testing 
involves a wide variety of antigens, adjuvants, formulations 
as well as potential safety concerns. Advances in vaccinology 
have resulted in the development of many new vaccines to pre-
vent both existing and emerging diseases, the improvement of 
existing vaccines, and new paradigms have been developed to 
rapidly respond to pandemic to meet global health needs with-
out compromising safety.
8.7.10  Medical Device Development
The development of medical devices encompasses a wide 
range of products from simple bandages to prosthesis and 
drug delivery systems. In the past several decades, the role 
of medical devices has greatly expanded in the United States 
and Europe with approximately 5,600 and 32,000 medical 
technology companies, respectively [370,371] Advancements 
in technology and biomedical engineering have enabled 
vast improvements in the “next generation” of medical 
devices including miniaturized leadless cardiac pacemak-
ers and transcatheter-delivered heart valves [372]. Section 
201 (hours) of the U.S. Food, Drug and Cosmetic Act [373] 
broadly defines medical devices as “an instrument, appa-
ratus, implement, machine, contrivance, implant, in vitro 
reagent or other similar related article, including a compo-
nent part, or accessory” which:
•	 is recognized in the official National Formulary, or 
the United States Pharmacopoeia, or any supplement 
to them,
•	 is intended for use in the diagnosis of disease or other 
conditions, or in the cure, mitigation, treatment, or 
prevention of disease, in man or other animals, or
•	 is intended to affect the structure or any function of 
the body of man or other animals, and which does 
not achieve its primary intended purposes through 
chemical action within or on the body of man or 
other animals and
•	 does not achieve its primary intended purposes 
through chemical action within or on the body of 
man or other animals and which is not dependent 
upon being metabolized for the achievement of 
its primary intended purposes…
In addition, medical devices are generally classified into differ-
ent categories based on the level of potential harm (e.g., inva-
sive versus noninvasive) and risk to the patient (e.g., short-term

379
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
TABLE 8.16
Recommendations and Guidelines for Nonclinical Development of Vaccines
Animal model
Relevant species chosen to develop immune response to vaccine antigen and 
similarities to human anatomy and physiology. Only one relevant species is 
generally required since metabolism and kinetics are not involved in the 
biological response. Typically rodent, rabbit, NHP.
Number of animals
Usually 5/sex/group in rabbits or NHPs and 10/sex/group in rodents for both 
terminal and recovery phases. Cohorts of mice can be added for interim clinical 
pathology or immunogenicity evaluation.
Reactogenicity/local tolerance
Draize scoring for erythema and edema should be done prior to each dose and 
for at least 3 days following dosing. Injection site observations such as eschar 
and induration should be noted.
Clinical observations, body weight, and food consumption
Mortality, morbidity, and general health should be evaluated twice daily. Physical 
exams should be done once weekly at a minimum. Body weight and food 
consumption should be daily in rabbits but weekly in other species with body 
weights also collected 24–48 hours after each dose since these can be affected 
by activation of the immune response. The daily body weight and food 
consumption in rabbits are recommended and are designed to reduce/prevent 
gastrointestinal issues that frequently result in rapid decline in health and 
mortality.
Body temperature
Recommend to measure by implanted microchip at 6 (and sometimes 10 hours) 
and 24 hours after each dose and daily if still elevated. 
Ophthalmic examinations
Prior to dosing and then during the week preceding terminal necropsy. Evaluate 
animals in the recovery groups prior to necropsy if findings noted at terminal 
necropsy. Some recent studies have not included these exams.
Hematology, clinical chemistry, and urinalysis
At a minimum should be done prior to dosing, then 1–3 days following the first 
and last dose and prior to the recovery necropsy. Occasionally clinical 
pathology evaluation may be done after each dose. This differs from nonclinical 
studies of a small or large molecule that measure these prior to the first dose 
and prior to necropsies (large animals) or prior to necropsies only (small 
animals). Recent studies have not included urinalysis especially when the test 
system is the rabbit. 
Acute-phase reactants
C-reactive protein should be evaluated in rabbits and monkeys prior to dosing, 
within 48 hours of the first dose, one week following the first dose, and prior to 
each necropsy at a minimum. A panel of coagulation variables should be 
evaluated at the same timepoints. In rats and mice, the acute-phase reactant to 
be evaluated should include alpha2-macroglobulin at a minimum. Haptoglobin 
can be included studies using rats and mice.
Immunogenicity
Samples for evaluation of antibody formation should be collected prior to dosing 
and prior to each necropsy at a minimum to confirm the selection of relevant 
animal model, determine protective immune response, and confirm exposure. 
May also be done prior to each dose to evaluate the increase in antibody titer 
with repeated dosing. The assays should be qualified but may not need to be 
validated. Examples include specific ELISA, hemagglutination, and plaque 
neutralization assays.
Necropsy
Gross observations, collect tissues as per protocol, gross lesions, organ weights.
Histopathology
Special attention is given to the evaluation of draining lymph nodes, thymus, 
spleen, bone marrow, and injection sites. The choice of tissues to examine 
depends on the vaccine and nonclinical/clinical experience. For example, 
vaccines given by inhalation should also include primary and secondary 
bronchi, bronchus-associated lymphatic tissues, mediastinal lymph nodes, and 
oropharynx and vaccines given by the intranasal route should include multiple 
levels of the nasal cavity, the olfactory and other cranial nerves, the cribriform 
plate, and brain.
Special immunologic investigations and other assays
Precipitation of immune complex, autoimmunity; mechanistic investigation may 
be indicated by nonclinical and/or clinical responses. A panel of cytokines may 
be considered. A standard cytokine panel may be included along with 
flow-cytometry but these assays are not typically validated.
Reproductive and developmental toxicity
Primarily indicated if the patient population includes WOCBP and should be 
included in the IND package if applicable. This should also be done if maternal 
immunization is required to protect the fetus or neonate from infection. The 
types of studies are decided case-by-case basis. The ICH S5(R2) and S5(R3) 
provide guidance. The choice of species is important depending on the timing 
of placental transfer.
