380
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
versus long-term use) [374]. In the United States, the FDA 
classifies medical devices into three tiers: Class A, low haz-
ard level (e.g., bandages); Class B, moderate hazard level (e.g., 
hypodermic needles or bone fixation plate); and Class C, high 
hazard level (e.g., heart valves). A comprehensive table of 
international medical device classifications is available in the 
mini review by Schuh and Funk [375].
Biomedical devices are devices used externally or inter-
nally for diagnostic purposes, like ECG electrodes, or to tem-
porarily or permanently preserve, restore, or augment tissue 
or organ structure and/or function as with titanium implants 
(metal), breast implants (silicone), absorbable and nonabsorb-
able suture material (polymers), and stents (collagen) [376]. 
Toxicological evaluation of these devices must include analy-
sis of the device itself, as well as the material from which the 
device is made or derived, these include [375]:
•	 biocompatibility testing of the biomaterials by in 
vivo implantation or other studies
•	 safety testing (local and systemic toxicology of the 
final finished form [FFF])
•	 efficacy testing of the FFF or clinical product
By definition, biomaterials can be a single element or a mixture 
or composite of elements, synthetic or derived, that contact tis-
sue or bodily fluids in order to preserve, restore or augment 
tissue structure and function [374]. Biomaterials commonly 
used include metals, silicone, polymers, and naturally derived 
biomaterials such as collagen.
According to the U.S. FDA, there are a number of prelimi-
nary in vitro and in vivo biocompatibility tests that may be 
used for biomaterial and medical device safety assessment. 
Although not all tests are required, initial findings may sup-
port the use of supplemental testing strategies [377]. Tests 
described in the Use of International Standard ISO documents 
are as follows [378]:
•	 Cytotoxicity: measures cell death, cytolysis, inhib-
ited cell growth or colony formation, among others.
•	 Genotoxicity: evaluates DNA effects, gene muta-
tions, chromosomal aberrations, etc.
•	 Sensitization: reviews for evidence of an inflamma-
tory response after initial exposure followed by a 
latency period and challenge exposure.
•	 Pyrogenicity: essentially looks for fever induction 
using a bacterial endotoxin test.
•	 Leachables and Extractables: Leachables are sub-
stances that can migrate or be extracted from a bioma-
terial or device under conditions that mimic clinical 
exposure. Extractables are substances released using 
extraction solvents or under conditions that mimic 
exaggerated clinical conditions.
•	 Degradation: includes degradation products that may 
be produced from various chemical reactions like 
hydrolysis or oxidative cleavage.
•	 Hemocompatibility: applies to any device or material 
that interfaces with blood and includes evaluation for 
hematology (hemolysis), coagulation, complement 
activation, platelet activity, and thrombogenicity 
(thrombosis).
Flow charts included in regulatory guidance documents 
provide a useful framework for study design development 
(Figures 8.18 and 8.19) [378].
While nonclinical safety assessment for medical devices 
is similar to that of drugs and biological therapeutics, there 
are notable differences to consider [379–381]. As earlier 
described in this chapter, there is a well-defined check list and 
path-to-market for drug discovery and development of small 
and large molecule pharmaceuticals; however, this is not the 
case for medical devices. Another key difference is that both 
safety and efficacy (e.g., performance) of the FFF is required 
for medical device evaluation. Unlike drugs and biologics, in 
which investigational new drug applications may begin clini-
cal trials before all of the nonclinical testing has been com-
pleted, all nonclinical safety evaluations of medical devices 
must be completed prior to any clinical trial. In addition, the 
intended performance and function of a medical device must 
be adequately demonstrated in order to proceed with clinical 
trials. Despite this difference, medical device improvements 
may be available to patients within 18–24 months, unlike the 
many years and sometimes decades-long drug development 
TABLE 8.16 (Continued)
Recommendations and Guidelines for Nonclinical Development of Vaccines
Genotoxicity
These are generally not needed for final vaccine formulation but may be required 
for novel adjuvants and excipients.
Carcinogenicity
Not required for vaccines but may be required for novel adjuvants and excipients.
Safety pharmacology
These are not normally required but may be included if nonclinical and/or 
clinical data suggest vaccine may affect the function of CNS, respiratory, 
cardiovascular, or renal physiology.
Kinetics
Normally not needed but should be considered on case-by-case basis when using 
novel adjuvants. The immunogenicity evaluation is considered proof of 
exposure.
Adjuvants
Adjuvant effects should be evaluated in nonclinical reactogenicity studies. For a 
new adjuvant toxicity studies of the adjuvant alone should be conducted.
Additives
New additives, with no toxicology data, should develop a toxicology profile.

Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
timelines for pharmaceutical and biotechnology products 
[379,380]. Medical device studies are often challenged by low 
animal numbers and paradoxically high time and dollar invest-
ment in study materials and results [381]. Typical animal mod-
els for medical device studies include rodents (mice and rats), 
dogs, minipigs, and nonhuman primates (e.g., cynomolgus 
macaque); however, large animals such as ruminants, larger 
breeds of pigs (e.g., Duroc), and laboratory animal species not 
typically used for medical device studies, such as rabbits and 
chinchillas, may be used if necessary [379,380]. A complete 
list of notable differences between drug safety assessment and 
medical device evaluation is available in the review article by 
Kirchoff [380].
In general, a number of important criteria must be addressed 
as part of the medical device evaluation [379] and include cyto-
toxicity, extractables and leachables, sensitization, irritation, 
hemocompatibility, genotoxicity, pyrogenicity (relating to bac-
terial endotoxin release), particulates and contaminants, local 
tissue tolerance (e.g., tissue reaction associated with implanta-
tion sites in soft tissues), acute and chronic toxicity, and man-
ufacturing/packaging sterilization and contaminants [379]. 
These and other criteria for medical device evaluation, such 
as reproduction and developmental toxicity, immunotoxicity 
and carcinogenicity, are addressed in the regulatory guidelines 
authored by the International Organization for Standardization 
(ISO), specifically the ISO 10993 series [375,379]. There are 
additional national and regional regulatory guidance docu-
ments that should be considered in conjunction with the ISO 
guidelines; these resources are also included in the mini 
review by Schuh and Funk [375].
Nonclinical safety assessment of pharmaceutical com-
pounds may sometimes result in no adverse findings, in which 
there is no detectable biochemical, macroscopic, or micro-
scopic change related to test-article administration or exposure 
[379]. However, for medical device studies, nearly all implant-
able biomedical devices produce a local tissue response, of 
varying degree, due to alterations of anatomical topography 
(e.g., device occupying and/or altering the physical space) or 
the implantation procedure (e.g., injection or surgical incision). 
In addition, local tissue responses may result from cellular/
biochemical reactions to or interactions with the biomateri-
als that comprise the medical device itself. While local tissue 
responses to medical devices are typical, it is important to note 
that these responses are not equivalent to adverse responses 
seen in general toxicology studies with small and large mol-
ecules and do not indicate abnormal changes or disease asso-
ciated with the medical device. Rather, these device-related 
tissue responses may be associated with tissue remodeling 
essential for device efficacy [379]. For example, osteocon-
duction (the ability of a bone implant to support bone growth 
on or within the implant) and osteointegration (the formation 
of bone directly on an implant surface without fibrous tissue 
involvement between new bone and the implant surface) rep-
resent essential host tissue responses that ensure long-term sta-
bility of bone implants [379,382]. Tissue responses for medical 
device implants generally produce classic features associated 
FIGURE 8.18  Biocompatibility Evaluation Flow Chart from FDA, Figure 1 [73]. The flow chart is provided to illustrate how one might proceed with 
a biocompatibility evaluation.

382
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
with wound healing and tissue integration specific to the 
tissue compartment of the implantation site and the type of 
medical device used. Thus, local tissue responses to medical 
devices are expected and should be recognized, interpreted, 
and described as such as part of the safety assessment. This 
represents another important difference between medical 
device and drug safety studies in which most implant studies 
will have microscopic findings of note, but drug studies may 
sometimes yield “no test-article related findings” [379].
Medical device safety assessment often involves complex 
macroscopic and microscopic pathology interpretations that 
are tailored to each specific device and study. As such, early 
communication between the pathologist, toxicologist, sponsor 
and/or regulatory agency is critical, especially for establish-
ment of agreement on study end points and objectives in the 
study design phase [379,381]. Study endpoints refer to the mea-
surement of a specific parameter and can be semiquantitative 
(e.g., a histology grading scheme for the degree of inflamma-
tion) or qualitative (histomorphometry to measure new bone 
formation). For medical device studies, emphasis is typically 
placed on the macroscopic, or gross, observations associated 
with the device in situ and is the source of critically impor-
tant data capture and documentation. High-resolution digital 
photography and formalin-fixed paraffin-embedded histology 
are traditionally used for medical device pathology evaluation 
but may be supplemented with plastic microground sections 
in cases where devices cannot be cut from paraffin-embed-
ded tissue blocks (e.g., metallic compounds or bone implants) 
[381]. It is highly advantageous for the medical device patholo-
gist and other technical experts, for example, toxicologist and 
study scientist, be included in the study design process; they 
have knowledge of the study protocol requirements and appli-
cable regulatory guidelines based on the device type, stage of 
development, complexity of the device, and associated clinical 
risk factors. Proper collection and handling of the study data 
are essential and must include the raw data: all gross observa-
tions (necropsy and tissue dissection/sectioning notes), gross 
photographs, image files (e.g., radiographs), and glass slides 
as well as data that can be recaptured (e.g., photomicrographs) 
[380–382].
Medical device safety studies are unique to the medical 
device or class of medical device. Medical device development 
continues to expand and evolve as a result of rapid techno-
logical and biomedical advancements. Evaluation of medical 
device safety and efficacy is highly regulated, and data driven, 
thus, documentation and complete and consistent data collec-
tion are critical. Determining the best approach for medical 
device evaluation and interpretation involves early commu-
nication between the pathologist, study director, sponsor, and 
the relevant regulatory agency, prior to the start of the study. 
Medical device pathology requires a specialized approach tai-
lored to the specific device and objectives of the study [383].
8.7.11  Genotoxic (Mutagenic) Impurities
Impurities are defined as providing no therapeutic benefit, but 
having the potential to cause adverse effects. Genotoxic impu-
rities, specifically, refer to substances demonstrating genotox-
icity, or the potential for genotoxicity (e.g., Ames test positive). 
It is important to understand the relationship between the 
FIGURE 8.19  Biocompatibility Evaluation Flow Chart from FDA, Figure 2 [73]. This flow chart is a continuation of the decision tree for 8.18 when 
redirected to Figure 2 for biocompatibility evaluation.

383
Toxicologic Assessment of Pharmaceutical, Medical Device and Biotechnology Products
genotoxicity and mutagenicity terminology. Genotoxicity is a 
broad description of any damage to genetic material, regard-
less of mechanism. Mutagenicity, on the other hand, implies a 
permanent change in the quantity or structure of genetic mate-
rial in a cell/organism. All mutagens can be considered geno-
toxic, but not all genotoxicants can be considered mutagens 
[384]. Impurities in drug substances and products are broadly 
addressed in the ICH Q3 Guidance documents [385–388]. 
These guidelines establish the qualification of impurities using 
standard toxicological approaches and the assessment of risk. 
The ICH Q3 topics are somewhat vague on the handling of 
genotoxic impurities and do not address control or assessment 
of impurities prior to marketing applications.
Impurities, either genotoxic or nongenotoxic, occur in the 
manufacturing and storage process involved with pharmaceu-
ticals, they are generally less well defined in the early stages 
of development than they are as a product approaches market-
ing. Genotoxic impurities in pharmaceuticals originate from 
starting materials and their impurities, process by-products, 
solvents, reagents, storage conditions, leachables, degradation 
products, and intermediates [389–391]. Specific toxicology 
limits for commonly used materials in pharmaceutical manu-
facture have been reviewed [392]. It is a regulatory expectation 
[393] and the responsibility of the manufacturer to determine 
the actual and possible impurities in a product and to develop 
strategies to control these at levels as low as reasonably prac-
ticable. Control strategies include redesigning the synthetic 
process, changing process parameters, understanding whether 
an impurity will be formed, and conducting a bacterial test 
of mutagenicity to confirm or dispute genotoxicity/mutagenic-
ity [391]. Muller et al. [394] have published a pharmaceutical 
industry approach to determine, test, and control potentially 
genotoxic impurities. In this approach they propose the use of 
a staged Threshold of Toxicological Concern (TTC), recom-
mending a range of acceptable daily intake based on a range 
of use from ≤1 month to >12 months. They also categorized 
impurities into five classes [394,395]:
•	 Impurities known to be genotoxic (mutagenic) and 
carcinogenic
•	 Impurities known to be genotoxic but with unknown 
carcinogenic potential
•	 Alerting structure, unique, and of unknown geno-
toxic (mutagenic) potential
•	 Alerting structure; related to the active pharmaceuti-
cal ingredient (API)
•	 No alerting structure, or sufficient evidence for the 
absence of genotoxicity
The TTC history and use in risk assessment have been reviewed 
by Kroes et al. [396,397]. The determination of genotoxic 
potential is outlined in ICH S2(R1) [281] and includes a bacte-
rial gene mutation assay, an in vitro cytogenetic assay, and an 
in vivo test for genotoxicity, for example, chromosomal aberra-
tion. Mueller et al also provide a flowchart for the qualification 
of each class of impurity [394].
As mentioned previously, the ICH Q3A and Q3B docu-
ments [385,386] only provide limited guidance on genotoxic 
impurities. They also do not apply to new drug products in the 
clinical research phase of drug development. As a result, ICH 
has issued the ICH M7(R1) guidance document [395]. This 
document provides specific guidance on the genotoxic/muta-
genic potential of impurities and includes products in clinical 
development. In assessing the genotoxic/mutagenic potential 
of a material in a drug substance or drug product, the actual 
and possible impurities must be identified and the potential for 
toxicity is evaluated by in vitro, in silico, and in vivo testing 
[398]. ICH M7(R1) classifies impurities relative to mutagenic 
and carcinogenic potential and provides guidance on control 
actions. The basic test for genetic toxicity or mutagenic poten-
tial is the bacterial reverse mutation assay (Ames test); ICH M7 
recommends some specific assays to evaluate the relevance of 
the in vitro mutagenicity analysis, including transgenic muta-
tion, Pig-a, micronucleus, unscheduled DNA synthesis, Comet 
[395]. It describes the type of post-approval changes that will 
initiate a re-evaluation of mutagenic potential, for example, 
synthetic route, reagents, solvents, etc. Interestingly, while 
the M7 Guidance acknowledges the acceptability of a TTC-
based acceptable intake limits, it also provides the perspective 
that TTC is a highly hypothetical concept and should not be 
regarded as a realistic indication of actual risk [395].
The ICH M7 document should be read carefully by toxi-
cologists, process and analytical chemists engaged in the drug 
development process, paying specific attention to the Notes 
and Appendices. The impact of the requirements for genotoxic 
impurities has been reviewed [399,400] and include additional 
toxicology resources for in silico assessments and genetic toxi-
cology testing plus expert safety assessments.
8.8  Conclusion
The future engagement of toxicologists in drug development 
decisions appears to be very promising. Physicians and patients 
continue to expect more efficacious and safer medications, 
developed more quickly and less expensively. Basic research 
efforts in biochemistry, physiology, and pharmacology have 
allowed the more precise characterization of receptors and the 
normal and perturbed sequelae of receptor binding, thus con-
tinuing to stimulate the development of more specific, potent 
modulators of cellular functions. The etiologies of human dis-
eases are becoming better understood, thanks to the techno-
logic ability to elucidate their characteristics at the molecular 
level. This has resulted in the potential to therapeutically mod-
ify the disease process at its origin: the human genome. These 
molecular approaches have already resulted in the develop-
ment of agents that are highly species specific, and the use of 
these techniques to elucidate normal and pathological cellular 
function will only continue to escalate. The development of 
NBEs has already challenged the established norms of safety 
assessment. Consider the example of an agent that has shown 
selectivity for modifying the activity of a human-specific 
enzyme critical to a pathological process. Although traditional 
animal studies are likely to be predictive of toxicity that is 
unrelated to the pharmacology of the compound, they may not 
be useful for the prediction of adverse findings relative to the 
action of the drug at the human-specific target. Thus, not only

384
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
may data generated from classical animal studies imperfectly 
predict toxic responses to highly specific human therapy but 
also the information may be irrelevant or misleading. A major 
concern is whether modification of this enzyme activity, in the 
only responsive species (humans), might result in unantici-
pated, severe toxicity, and how can this best be predicted prior 
to the initiation of clinical trials.
It is unlikely that the classical/traditional tools/approaches 
of toxicology will be sufficient to ensure the human safety of 
the new highly specific therapeutics forthcoming from sophis-
ticated technologies. It would, however, be unwise to think 
that the strong foundation in classical toxicologic assessment 
and risk/benefit analysis has no place in the future of drug 
development; it is all about balance and the ability to apply 
new approaches based on a strong understanding of estab-
lished procedures.
The future direction of discovery research suggests that 
industrial and regulatory toxicologists will need to collaborate 
more closely in the design of the toxicology studies supporting 
registration; ultimately, these studies may have to be consid-
ered on a case-by-case basis. Indeed, the current guidelines 
resulting from global harmonization efforts repeatedly empha-
size the need for defending the scientific rationale supporting 
the design of proposed toxicologic assessments. Although 
these guidelines are viewed as much more flexible than the 
country-specific regulations of the past, they also place a 
greater burden on toxicologists relative to defending the rel-
evance of their studies. The era of checking the “tox” box has 
come closed.
Another challenge on the horizon concerns the need to 
improve the efficiency of the drug development process with-
out compromising its quality. Currently, the drug develop-
ment and approval processes, in many cases, are taking longer 
and costing more than ever before. More stringent regulatory 
requirements have resulted in the conduct of more studies 
(in both animals and humans) that take more time and cost 
more money. These costs are passed on to the patient, who 
ultimately must also compensate for the resultant decreased 
market life, due to patent length restrictions, of the registered 
product. Furthermore, the use of large numbers of experimen-
tal animals remains a concern from both the ethical and finan-
cial points of view. One approach to solving these dilemmas 
is to ensure that the toxicology studies conducted meet the 
needs of the regulatory agency, the physician, and ultimately 
the patient. As suggested earlier, this can be most efficiently 
accomplished by early and routine interactions between the 
industry, physicians, patients, and regulatory authorities, espe-
cially in cases where the agent under development represents a 
unique therapeutic approach.
The application of omics technologies, such as genom-
ics (toxico- or pharmaco-genomics), proteomics, and meta-
bonomics, is expected to enhance the toxicology toolbox 
for evaluation of drug safety. Proteomics, the analysis of 
protein expression patterns, and metabonomics, the evalua-
tion of metabolite profiles, are positive additions to the drug 
development toolbox. Toxicogenomics (or pharmacogenom-
ics) has, perhaps, the greatest potential to affect nonclinical 
safety assessment [225]. Toxicogenomics focuses on the study 
of differential gene expression (DGE) as an adaptation to 
chemical or environmental stress. A basic assumption is that 
DGE underlies all drug-induced toxic events, with the possible 
exception of very rapid cell death [275,276]. A goal of toxi-
cogenomics is to identify gene expression patterns that predict 
potential toxicity [401,402]. The identification of a causal rela-
tionship between DGE and delayed manifestations of frank 
toxicity can facilitate early drug development. It is anticipated 
that toxicogenomics will be increasingly integrated into all 
phases of drug development, particularly in mechanistic and 
predictive toxicology and biomarker identification [403]. The 
FDA has published a guidance document on submission of 
pharmacogenetic data [404]. Within the guidance, the FDA 
acknowledges that the toxicology database required to support 
clinical trials and marketing of drugs is well established. Any 
proposal for substitution of new animal genomics safety test-
ing will involve the international scientific and drug develop-
ment committees [404].
The major role of the toxicologist as a mechanistic scientist 
will continue to be enhanced. For the reasons discussed previ-
ously, the design of toxicology studies and the interpretation of 
toxicology data will become increasingly more sophisticated, 
requiring a broad knowledge base in a variety of other scien-
tific disciplines and a strong foundation in classical toxicology 
approaches [221]. Elucidation of the mechanisms responsible for 
observed toxicities would improve the ability to achieve the tra-
ditional, ultimate purpose of the discipline of toxicology: appro-
priate extrapolation of these data to humans [111]. Achievement 
of this goal will surely become more challenging but also more 
exciting, as the future approaches to drug development unfold.
The development of vaccines, related to the global pan-
demic of 2020, has moved forward considerably. In the past, 
it could take more than 10 years to develop a vaccine. Today, 
with new techniques, the vaccine approach to COVID-19 was 
developed, tested, and approved very rapidly. The U.S. FDA 
even initiated new rules for meetings related to this critical 
therapeutic approach. CGT products have also introduced 
challenges to the drug development paradigm, especially 
related to the choice of relevant species.
The development of medical devices, while having some 
similarities to the drug development paradigm, also introduces 
new challenges related to surgical procedures, defining the 
safety of device materials and proving that the devices work 
as designed prior to entering clinical trials. The toxicologist 
working in the medical device area engages with a broader 
range of regulatory guidance and departures from the tradi-
tional drug development IND through Phase I–III approach in 
providing evidence of safety and of efficacy.
While the established knowledge base still provides essen-
tial advantages to the toxicologist, the need to identify and 
validate new approaches in the drug development paradigm is 
clearly the future.
